3.7
Kamstrup data record. The upper plot shows the forward water temperature measured before the
shut loop in the apartment. The next plot presents the return water temperature measured in the
outlet manifold before the cold water passes the shunt loop and exits to district heating. The
consecutive plots depict the temperature difference between supply and return, volumetric water
ﬂow and power consumed by the apartment based on the usage of water for space heating.

==PAGE:12==
vi
List of Figures
3.12 The simulation of the hysteresis effect. It can be seen that the jumps in x happen at moments
when µ crosses bifurcation points ±0.5 and the memory effect is present. The solution x has
been shifted by 1 and scaled by 0.5 to show that by proper tuning, it can get very close to the
physical heat control value changing in the range 0 to 1. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
48
3.13 The top plot shows residuals of TfTr model. The second plot is the average temperature -
the model’s output and the one step ahead predicted output. The consecutive two plots are
respectively solar irradiance and ambient temperature. The bottom plot shows the input of the
one-zone-apartment model constructed using logical OR operation. It is so called heating control
value send from thermostat to valve drive actuator.

Preface
The dissertation is submitted as a partial fulﬁlment of the requirements to achieve Master of Science in
Engineering in Automation and Robot Technology at Center for Electric Power and Energy department at
Technical University of Denmark.
The project was carried out starting from the 1st of February 2017 to the 22nd of August 2017 under the
supervision of Senior Researcher Yi Zong and PhD student Morten Herget Christensen.
The thesis aims at meeting objectives of the tasks - modelling and optimal control of smart energy
building, of EnergyLab Nordhavn project supported by Energy Technology Development and Demonstration
Programme (EUDP) and is accomplished in cooperation with Balslev Consulting Engineers A/S, one of the
12 project participants.
Jakub Krzysztof Rodek (s151836)

==PAGE:18==
==PAGE:19==
Abstract
Energy efﬁcient, air-tight buildings with district heating supplied radiant ﬂoor heating systems are
gaining on popularity in Denmark. The general public is becoming more aware of its advantages to the
environment compared to traditional solutions like central heating involving the combustion of fossil fuels.
The dissertation provides an insight into modelling and identiﬁcation of such systems from the control
point of view. Two data sets are used in the thesis: one is coming from the execution of three short duration
experiments in one of the apartments; the other one is the operational data from another apartment. The
latter reﬂects the normal use of the place by the owners meaning that the temperature set-points were set in
the rooms according to user’s preferences. The data sets are analysed from the perspective of deriving a
feasible model. Linear and non-linear models are considered where the non-linearity originates from the
need to estimate the return water temperature. The estimation equation is a non-linear function of the mass
ﬂow rate. The linear model of the room’s dynamics is performing better than the proposed non-linear models
of raising complexity.
Two modelling approaches are investigated. The grey-box approach is tested on the experimental data in
the effort to derive one-zone model of the apartment and the black-box approach is using operational data
set to derive model of one zone being part of another apartment.
The study proposes a PID controller as thermostat model, controlling the Radiant ﬂoor heating system
(RFHS), to maintain linearity of the resulting closed-loop model and to be able to design a linear Model
Predictive Control (MPC) supervisory controller. That is a simpliﬁcation which has its drawbacks. For
example it allows for negative water ﬂow rate having a cooling effect in the system. That is physically
impossible in a system with district heating because the ﬂow cannot be reversed and there is no cooling unit
in the investigated building.
An advanced control strategy - Economic Model Predictive Control (EMPC) is used in a hierarchical
control to optimize temperature set-points to local thermostatic controllers. The task of EMPC is to minimize
the cost of heat consumption. It does so by incorporating forecasts of heat price, from an aggregator, solar
irradiance and ambient temperature. Based on the model of the apartment/room and disturbances acting
on it, the EMPC predicts the future indoor temperature evolution and optimizes temperature set-points to
the local controllers to achieve cost savings without violating any thermal comfort preferences or actuator
limitations. The heat price variation is reﬂecting the energy demand hence the optimal scheduling helps
alleviate the peak load on the distribution network of district heating.
Another concept addressed in the dissertation is the notion of a so called smart start. The principle is to
delay the start of a non-thermal household appliance to make use of low electricity prices. This again helps
to balance supply and demand in the electricity grid. For Distribution System Operators (DSO) it means that,
if more households would use this concept there would be no need to scale up the distribution network for
xiii
==PAGE:20==
xiv
ABSTRACT
high peak demand because it would be lowered as the number of smart start implementations increases.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1
Problem formulation
Model predictive control is an advanced control method used in process industry and recently it attracts
applications in smart energy systems. It is based on a system model which should be relatively simple to
minimize computational overhead but detailed enough to capture the general trend of the system’s dynamical
behaviour. The use of the receding horizon strategy and the feedback from measurements makes the method
very effective in respecting the system’s constraints such as actuator and user deﬁned constraints like thermal
comfort limits on indoor temperature.
The focus of this master thesis lies in the scope of EnergyLab Nordhavn project which aims at integrating
large degree of renewable energy sources and enhance its operation by the use of advanced control techniques
and creating communication link between energy markets and prosumers to demonstrate how electricity and
heating, energy-efﬁcient buildings and electric transport can be integrated into an intelligent, ﬂexible and
optimized energy system.
The main objective of the master project is to develop an optimal predictive controller for heating systems
(such as ﬂoor heating) and appliances (washing machines and dish washers) scheduling for a residential
building - Frihavns Tårnet, located at Nordhaven, Copenhagen. The underlying principle for it is to employ
forecasts of different factors having impact on the local environment conditions in the building, like weather:
solar irradiance, ambient temperature; intermittent wind power production to enhance the living conditions
and minimize energy usage.
The biggest challenge for the thesis is anticipated to be extraction and the use of time series of data
logged in the apartments by means of various sensors. The data stream needs to be analyzed, interpreted and
processed for the identiﬁcation part. Two modelling methodologies will be investigated. The ﬁrst one is a
grey box, where partial knowledge of the system structure in the form of Stochastic Differential Equations is
combined with the physical data to ﬁnd a model of the system/apartment. The second one is a black box
approach which name stems from the limited knowledge of the system. It relies mainly on the information
extracted from the data to estimate model parameters. Identiﬁcation method needs to be chosen to match the
nature of the data, and to make accurate and at the same time simple enough model suitable for predictive
control.
The main stress lies in the development of a linear model predictive controller for space heating of the
smart apartments. The emphasis will be on single apartment of the building which consists of several rooms,
each with its own ﬂoor heating system and supplied with hot water from district heating system. The rooms
called zones are controlled separately with respect to the temperature, so the owners of the apartment can set
different temperature set-points in each of the zones. As future extension to the master thesis a graphical
7
==PAGE:30==
8
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
user interface could be made allowing the inhabitants to change the temperature settings in the zones and
communicating to them various parameters back like how much energy savings they can make by allowing
the temperature, for the next 24-hour period, to vary within the proposed limits.
In the project linear MPC will be implemented in MATLAB and simulated. Next the performance of the
controller will be assessed.
Smart start of appliances issue will be addressed as well. In the project a smart control of dish washer
and washing machine will be implemented, where the decision burden of the start time of these machines
will be made by predictive controller based on the price signal received from an aggregator (e.g. from CEE
data house) or based on services required for wind balancing.
2
State-of-the-art
Since the model is an essential part of MPC, an extensive literature review on modelling is a key part of
the thesis. Some of the most relevant articles for this part follow.
Since the water decreases its temperature as it travels in the distribution system, through the transmission
of the energy into the ﬂoor slab, it results in a non-uniform temperature distribution of the ﬂoor surface. In
the article S Hwan Cho (2012), a modelling focus lies mainly in the ﬂoor dynamics. The room model is
represented as a ﬁrst order Ordinary Differential Equation (ODE). The ﬂoor slab is modelled as a number of
vertical layers, one of which include the tube with hot water. Other layers are gypsum, air, concrete and
insulation, below the tube layer. Above it there are mortar and wood layers. The pipe loops are casted into
ﬂoor along the length of the room. The distribution system is modelled as temperature nodes of each layer
where it is assumed that a section of the tube placed along the length of the room, L is uniform. Using
this approach, the number of state will be an integer multiple of 12 ODEs. The exact number depends on
the tube pitch, P and the width of room W. The authors proposed a predictive PI control scheme where
local PI feedback controllers receive optimized set-points from supervisory controller or Programmable
Logic Controller (PLC). There is one PI controller per zone and one for gas-ﬁred boiler. The water supply
temperature is controlled by PI controller by adjusting the boiler’s combustion rate. The optimization of
the set-points for local controllers is an approach taken in this dissertation. This minimizes the need for
changes in the current system set-up. Another inspiration taken from the article, as will become apparent in
the project, is that the thermostat controller is modelled as a PID controller instead of hysteresis to maintain
linearity of the combined model.
In Honglian Thybo and Thybo (2007) the ﬂoor is divided into sub- and top-layers and a distributed model
of the sub-ﬂoor is presented which allows to calculate its top layer’s temperature. The top-ﬂoor is represented
as a thermal resistance in the model. The room model is static because as argued in the article its dynamics
are much slower than the water circuit’s. As mentioned, the authors focus on the accurate modelling of the
fastest dynamics in the system. They divide the sub-ﬂoor into m number of slices along the direction of
the water pipe to model the loss of temperature. Aside from that each such a slice is radially divided into n
circular layers around the center of the water pipe. Moreover the authors propose a control scheme which
targets the slow dynamics of the concrete ﬂoor and tries to increase its control performance. That is achieved
by two controllers connected in cascade. The inner controller regulates the concrete temperature which is
estimated from the water temperature. The outer loop controller regulates the room temperature. The tests
proved that supply water temperatures higher than the ﬂoor damage point can be achieved as the input to the
ﬂoor heating system without causing any harm to the ﬂoor structure by making sure the estimated concrete
temperature stays lower than that limit. The accurate model of the ﬂoor dynamics presented in the article is
too complex to be considered in the thesis. Instead a simple low order model is seeked after.
==PAGE:31==
3. CONTRIBUTION
9
The article Zhenyu Yang (2007) presents a linear second-order model for the ﬂoor and room dynamics
which is simple enough to be applicable to MPC. The authors describe the indoor climate control using
RFHS in conjunction with heat pump. Different control strategies are compared in the study leading to
conclusion that PID controller with preﬁltering is the best performing one with the smallest temperature
overshoot and least sensitivity to disturbances among other quality measures. The model presented by the
authors is used in this project with some modiﬁcations.
Summarizing the literature was an inspiration to
• replace the local non-linear hysteretic controllers with linear PID controllers,
• develop a simple model of the RFHS for the control purpose,
• focus on slow ﬂoor’s dynamics rather than the relatively fast room’s dynamics while extending the
complexity of models.
3
Contribution
The contribution of the thesis lies within a design of a linear Economic Model Predictive controller for
an indoor climate regulation in a newly built, well insulated "smart home" with RFHS supplied by district
heating. The focus is on a single apartment in a 13-ﬂoor apartment building in Nordhavn area, Copenhagen.
Due to the fact that the work on the building is still in the commencement stage a model of the
thermodynamics of the apartment was not estimated accurately and needed to be further improved.
The second contribution is a design of an algorithm for a so called "smart start" of household appliances.
A physical implementation is already in place. The only missing part is the "logic" of the controller. In
concept there should be a single MPC controller making decisions about thermostats setpoints and initiating
the start of appliances.
4
Thesis organisation
The thesis is organised as follows. In the System description chapter 2, the current KNX conﬁguration and
local control architecture are presented. In chapter 3 - Modelling and System Identiﬁcation, the information
about identiﬁcation experiment is detailed. Apart from that it is shown how the collected data needed
to be conditioned before applying an identiﬁcation algorithm. Two modelling approaches are used and
results from them are validated. In chapter 4 an EMPC is formulated using the black box model of one
zone. A non-standard formulation is detailed using predictive Kalman Filter (KF) to accommodate the state
estimation of the model with the direct feed-through term form the input to the output. Simulation results
follow. In chapter 5 the smart start of household appliances is covered with the simulation results for both
single-task and multi-task operation.
==PAGE:32==
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2
System description
This chapter presents an overview of one of the apartments in Frihavns Tårnet building from its structural
point of view as well as the control system currently deployed to regulate the indoor climate. Firstly a
communication protocol for smart homes will be shortly introduced together with the software to conﬁgure
the communication system. Details of the current conﬁguration will be presented. Afterwards the current
control system will be explained. All the sensors, control actuators and communication systems are ABB’s
products.
1
KNX communication infrastructure
1.1
KNX protocol - based communication
The data communication in the building is based on KNX protocol. Each apartment has standalone,
separated from other apartments, KNX line. Due to legal issues it is not allowed to make one KNX system
for the entire building. The communication interfaces to individual apartments are KNX/IP gateways. They
are not interconnected.
KNX protocol deﬁnes different ways of accessing the KNX bus. Backbone line can be based on:
• TP - Twisted Pair
• PL - Power Line
• IP - Internet Protocol
In the project communication with KNX bus happens through KNX IP router (IPR/S 2.1 ) and hence the
backbone line is chosen as IP.
Data are transferred over TCP/IP network to Technical University of Denmark (DTU). There is a DTU
server acting as an interface to DTU PowerLab Data Warehouse planned to be deployed by September 2017.
The interface is listening to KNX buses and fetches the data. Until the warehouse is ﬁnished the data are
stored temporarily on the interface server.
The physical layer of Open Systems Interconnection (OSI) model is two-core twisted pair cable. KNX
bus requires a 30V DC power, in order to operate optimally. The power supply module is SV/S 30.640.3.1.
The supply current must not exceed 640 mA. It is estimated that each KNX component uses up to 10 mA.
Therefore maximum of 64 devices can be supplied by the power supply. It is also maximum number of
devices that can be connected to single KNX line as speciﬁed in the protocol.
Programming of KNX system is done through USB module - USB/S1.1 which constitutes an interface
between KNX bus and a computer running Engineering Tool Software (ETS) which is a manufacturer
11
==PAGE:34==
12
CHAPTER 2. SYSTEM DESCRIPTION
independent conﬁguration software tool for designing and conﬁguring building control installations with
KNX systems. ETS runs under Windows platform.
1.2
KNX Conﬁguration in ETS5
ETS version 5 is utilized in Nordhavn project to conﬁgure KNX system. It creates logical connections
between devices which are meant to communicate with each other. Each device on the network has its own
unique address. A device can have many communication channels designated with a group address and
reserved for exchange of a speciﬁc information. Those channels can be seen in ETS5 in the "Topology" view.
Similarly all group addresses are listed in "Group Addresses" view. There is a set of radiator valves in each
apartment controlling the water ﬂow rate in the thermal zones. There is one thermostatic controller called
Betjening Panels in Danish (BP) per radiator valve. The BPs are receiving temperature either from built-in
internal temperature sensors or externally from presence detectors installed on the ceiling. For internal
measurements the actual temperature is transmitted to the KNX bus every 5 min or upon change in the
value larger than 0.1◦C and for external measurements it is the same except for the bathrooms where it is
respectively 5 min or 0.5◦C. BPs in apartment 33 on the 10th ﬂoor with addresses 1.1.46 and 1.1.50 are
using internal temperature measurements but the values are not transmitted via the KNX bus. Each of BPs
outputs a heat control value. It is linked logically directly to one of the outputs of the valve drive actuator -
VAA/S 12, on group address 2/0/x and the value is sent cyclically to KNX bus every 1 min. In most of the
cases BPs are using internal temperature measurements but in three cases they are placed away from the
zone the temperature of which they control. In those instances they use measurements provided by presence
detectors instead. The logical links are presented in the tables 2.1 and 2.2:

The tables 2.1 and 2.2 shows the temperature source for each of the thermostatic controller - BP. Four of

The location of the zones which they control is speciﬁed as well. The last column speciﬁes the valve drive

the temperature measurement. The last two BPs have "NA" for non-applicable because the temperature
==PAGE:35==
2. THE RADIANT FLOOR HEATING SYSTEM
13
measured internally by them is not available on KNX bus. Instead temperature measurements from presence
detector or CO2 sensor will be used as a very close counterpart of the controlled temperature. For that
reason the chosen sensor needs to be close to the respective BP. Columns "Ctrl. gr. addr." indicated group
address of the control signal sent from BP and "Status ctrl. val." indicates a status group address which is
the status of the valve drive actuator output. BPs are identiﬁed by its names, e.g. BP0501 means that it is
a thermostatic controller in room 5 and it has number 1. Similar notation is adopted for the sensors. The

Floor construction
The ﬂoor heating system is provided by Roth Nordic A/S company. The total loop length of the pipes
varies between 21 to 117 m in apartments. The pipe’s diameter is 20 mm. The pitch between pipes/tube

distribution is closer to uniform which for large zones and long pipes would not hold true. That is why some

2.3
Manual thermostatic valve
As can be seen in the drawing 2.3, each zone usually have one pipe loop with a dedicated electro-valve.
Big rooms (around 400m2) can have two sets. The supply water temperature comes from the ﬂat substation
in the basement. The temperature is being down-regulated by the manual thermostatic valve to 35◦C in
the inlet manifold before it is being redistributed to individual zones. It is a self regulating system. The
regulation happens on an apartment level. That means each apartment has its own manual thermostatic valve
regulating supply water temperature. The way the regulation works is that the manual thermostatic valve
taps the hot water into the ﬂoor piping circuit in a way to maintain the desired set-point. Whenever the
temperature is too high the valve closes leaving the water circulating in the closed loop circuit. If on the
other hand the temperature reaches a bottom level the valve opens and lets the hot water enter the circuit

2.4
Circulating pump
In the system there is also Grundfoss intelligent circulating pump, which was set to maintain constant
pressure. This pump in conjunction with electric valves can regulate the water ﬂow. It operates a follows:
whenever a zone thermostatic controller requests the corresponding valve/-s to close and hence to restrict the
effective cross-section at that point, the pressure pump detects extra difﬁculty in pushing the water around in
the pipes. It will react on it by lowering effectively the ﬂow to keep the pressure unchanged. At the point of
installation where the manual thermostatic valve is placed, there is a Kamstrup meter, measuring both the
supply and return water temperatures and the ﬂow rate. The pump is depicted in the ﬁgure 2.6.
Figure 2.6: The intelligent circulating pump

3. THE RADIANT FLOOR HEATING CONTROL SYSTEM

3
The radiant ﬂoor heating control system
A zone temperature is controlled using RFHS. Some of the relevant for the thesis system components
are described in the section. The ﬂoor heating system control loop constitutes:
• Control element/thermostat, 6128/28-84-500 and bus coupler, 6120/12-101-500
• Valve Drive Actuator, VAA/S 6.230.2.1
• Thermoelectric Valve Drive, TSA/K 230.2 2CDG 120 049 R0011
• Radiant electro-valves
• Intelligent pressure pump
• Floor-casted pipe spirals
• Manual valve controlling the forward water temperature
The bus coupler couples the control element/thermostat to the KNX system. The ﬁgure 2.7 shows the control
loop.
In the ﬁgure 2.8 there is the controller and converter/actuator (VAA/S). Update times are given as well.
Whenever a telegram with a signal is transmitted on KNX bus it is being transmitted through Internet and
logged by DTU server. For instance Tz, set signal is updated every 15 min or whenever there is a change in
the signal. One can see that update times for signals related to each other like zone temperature set-point and
measured zone temperature, differ from one another. A control error is calculated as the difference between
the signals and depending on the value of the signal the 2-point controller sends signal: “FULLY OPEN” or
“FULLY CLOSED” to the converter/actuator. The actuator converts the opening position [in %] requested
by the controller and represented on 1 byte to a voltage according to function shown in the bottom part of
the drawing. The VAA/S has 12 outputs but for convenience only one of them has been shown. Each output
is connected to TSA/K 230.2 which directly actuates one of the valves for one zone. The output signal is
Pulse Width Modulation (PWM) with the cycle of 3 min. Since the opening/closing time of valves is 3 min
and hence also the minimum time spent on either of the PWM states last that amount of time, the effective
output will be either ON or OFF at any period of 3 min. The thermostats in an apartment are connected
through KNX bus to the input of valve drive actuator. The actuator’s outputs are then connected with the
corresponding thermoelectric valve drives using wires, which are not part of the KNX system.
3.1
Thermostatic controller
Indoor temperature is regulated in a closed-loop to a speciﬁed set-point, zone-wise by thermostat (ABB
component - 6128/28-84-500). The thermostatic controller is a non-linear 2-point controller with hysteresis
of 1◦C. The control signal has length of 1 byte and can assume values 0% or 100%. That means if control
error being the difference between zone temperature set-point and measured room temperature is greater
than 0.5◦C the control signal will be 100%. Conversely if that difference is smaller than −0.5◦C the control
signal will be 0%. The temperature set-point to the module is sent to DTU data warehouse every 15 min or
upon a value change. The knowledge about it is very useful with regards to closed-loop system identiﬁcation.
Knowing the controller there is no need to identify it and an identiﬁcation procedure focuses only on the
controlled object which in the thesis is an apartment with a ﬂoor heating system. The control element
contains temperature sensor. There is one or two (for big rooms) of them per room. User can specify their
desired zone temperature in it. Control error is computed by subtracting the measured temperature. The
==PAGE:42==
20
CHAPTER 2. SYSTEM DESCRIPTION
Figure 2.7: Floor heating control loop
==PAGE:43==
3. THE RADIANT FLOOR HEATING CONTROL SYSTEM
21
Figure 2.8: A controller diagram for the ﬂoor heating system.
error is propagated through the controller and the resulting control signal is sent to ABB product (VAA/S
6.230.2.1) which is a passive element and does not contain any logic. It just converts the control signal from
0% – 100% valve opening command to duty cycle (PWM signal). The signal is passed on to thermally active
element (TSA/K) containing wax which melts under warm conditions and closes the radiant electro-valve
it controls. The approximate closing/opening times for the TSA/K is 3 min. The VAA/S converter is
programmable with ETS version 5 proprietary ABB software. Opening/closing times of the electro-valve
can be speciﬁed in the program as well.
3.2
Valve Drive Actuator
The Valve Drive Actuator, VAA/S 12.230.2.1 can control up to 12 electric radiator valves by using an
ON/OFF signal. Some of the most important information about it is listed here for a quick reference:
• 12 semiconductor outputs operated at 24. .. 230 V AC, 50/60 Hz; in the project it is 230 VAC control
signals sent to Thermoelectric Valve Drives in the form of binary output providing 230 VAC modulated
signal PWM. In other words VAA/S valve actuator sends binary (ON/OFF) control signal to TSAK/230.
The signal is PWM signal with fundamental harmonic being 230 V AC signal.
• It receives inputs from control panel (6128/28-84-500) through KNX bus connection terminals by
means of telegrams.
• For big apartments there are two of them.
• Input channels (6128/28-84-500 - zone temperature controller) to the valve actuator are coupled with
usually one TSAK 230.2 but for big rooms several of them can be assigned to one input channel. In
that case, all of them receive the same control signal.

The current settings in the Valve Drive Actuator opens the radiator valve when the received control value
as an OPEN/CLOSE – signal is at least 1% which corresponds to byte value around 3. The other option for
the format of the received control signal is PWM but it is not selected. Nevertheless in ETSv5 the cycle time
of PWM is speciﬁed to be 180 sec. This value is also the shortest time the controlled valves can open/close.
There is a possibility to operate VAA/S in two modes: manual or KNX. In manual operation the valves
the element is controlling can be switched on/off by means of buttons located on the device. Manual
operation is enabled in the device and reset to KNX operation happens after 30 min.
3.3
Thermoelectric Valve Drive
Characteristics of TSA/K element are:
• linear stroke versus time characteristic
• stroke (actuator travel) depends on the voltage applied - binary relation so either nominal 230 VAC
applied or 0 VAC.
• actuator travel - 4 mm
• operating voltage : 230 V AC +/ −10%, 50/60 Hz
3.4
Sensors and meters
The sensors installed in apartment are:
• Operating panel/thermostat with temperature sensor, controlling the air temperature in the speciﬁed
thermal zone in the apartment.
• Air quality sensor (LGS/A 1.1 )- Mounted just above the operating panel. It senses: CO2, temperature
and humidity.
• Presence sensor mounted on the ceiling.
• 2 analog inputs for measuring the water supply and return ﬂow temperature in the heating system
• Energy meter, B23 111-100
• Energy Module, EM/S3.16.1
The presence sensor has a built-in Passive Infrared Sensor (PIR). Apart from that it has the ability to sense
temperature. It is connected to the KNX system. Usually the built-in temperature sensors in operating panels
==PAGE:45==
4. DISTRICT HEATING
23
are incorporated in closed loop temperature control. In some places where the panels are mounted outside
of the zone, they control the current temperature is taken from PIR sensors in that zone. CO2 sensors are
not included in the control system because the ventilation system is centrally controlled and its intake air
temperature setpoint cannot be modiﬁed from value 21◦C. The Nordhaven project speciﬁes upper bound
for the CO2 level at 800 ppm but a regulator coupled to the sensors is providing only a value for a damper
position to achieve theoretical control of ventilating system. Energy meters are not connected directly to
KNX system. They are meant to be read directly by users. They measure the total energy usage in apartments.
Putting the most recent measurements of voltage, current, wattage on KNX bus and therefore enabling
remote energy reading, requires an auxiliary reading module (ZS/S 1.1). It is placed above the energy
meter’s Infrared (IR) transmitter. Energy Module measures active power, current, voltage and frequency.
The building has no solar irradiance sensors installed, measuring in the units of [W/m2]. Only Lux sensors
measuring illuminance, are installed in big apartments. There are usually two per apartment.
3.5
Actuators
Every electrical contact in the apartment can be controlled on the KNX bus by for example the module
SA/S12.16.5 which has 12 independent relay type outputs. Energy consumption of groups of devices
connected to the contacts is measured by energy module - EM/S 3.16.1 and the measurement is made
available on KNX bus. As an example all the relays in a single room can be controlled simultaneously by
one output of the actuator but the energy consumption of a few rooms in total is measured because they
are treated as a group. Another group could be contacts in walk-in, hall and laundry room or lights over
mirrors in bathroom 1 and 2. Summarizing, even though each contact is controlled separately the energy
consumption is measured for a group of lumped devices connected to different contacts. Lights can also be
controlled on KNX bus by a dimmer module like 4-channel 6197/12-101-500.
4
District heating
The ﬂoor heating system is supplied from HOFOR District Heating (DH). The DH in Copenhagen area
is combined with Combined Heat and Power (CHP) and waste incineration plants. In the future it is possible
to use Low Temperature District Heating (LTDH). According to information provided by HOFOR, LTDH
operates in a range between 50 −55◦C to 60 −70◦C for the forward water temperature and 25 −30◦C
to 40◦C for the return water temperature. Usually heat exchanger is used to decouple the DH side from
end-user side: space heating and Domestic Hot Water (DHW). In multi-storey buildings, which Frihavns
Tårnet is an example of, there are substations equipped with its own metering devices for keeping track of
the heat consumption of the entire building.
5
Centrally-controlled ventilation system
The ventilation system is centrally controlled meaning the air ﬂow is controlled to ﬁxed value for the
whole building - constant air volume (CAV). The system is located at the top of the building. It draws the
fresh air at the top and redistributes it to apartments. It consists of cross-ﬂow heat exchanger (in Danish:
krydsvarmeveksler) with estimated efﬁciency around 90% where the pipes for forward and return air are
running in parallel close to each other facilitating heat exchange. The fresh and cold outside air is heated up,
not mixed, by running the opposite direction to the warm, stale air from rooms. If still the temperature is too
low it could be heated up further by a district heating - based after-heater.
==PAGE:46==
24
CHAPTER 2. SYSTEM DESCRIPTION
Control of the ventilated air temperature is set currently at 21◦C. This is the air temperature before it
reaches the top ﬂoors apartment. It can be expected that it gets higher as it traverses the building structure
reaching bottom level apartments. It has been observed that 21◦C temperature could be too much because
less efﬁcient after-heater is being activated and ﬂoor heating is being left deactivated most of the time. The
temperature set-point that was proven optimal in other building in Nordhavn is 18◦C.
At the moment there is no data from the ventilation system and no access to its control system therefore
the ventilation system is not considered in this thesis.
If the temperature requested by users for the indoor air is not met by the ventilation system then the
district heating - based ﬂoor heating system is activated. Those are separate circuits. The DH one acts on the
indoor air temperature through much slower ﬂoor heating system. If the requested indoor temperature is low
enough that the ventilation system can take care of it itself then it happens much faster compared to the ﬂoor
heating system. That is undesirable operation because it undermines the purpose of ﬂoor heating system.
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 3
Modelling and system identiﬁcation
In the course of the project 3 different experiments, 1-day in duration, were conducted on the plant in
March 2017. Data from those experiments was used to estimate a model of the apartment. Also operational
data from April 2017 was used to identify a model of a single room. The analysis of both data sets is
presented in this chapter.
1
Experiment design
In the course of the project 3 different experiments, 1-day in duration, were conducted on the plant in
March 2017. Data from those experiments was used to estimate a model of the apartment. Also operational
data from April was used to identify a room model. The analysis of both data sets is presented in this chapter.
1.1
Closed-loop vs. open-loop identiﬁcation
As described in the previous chapter the RFHS system constitutes of radiant ﬂoor and a hysteretic
thermostat controlling it in closed loop. The parameters of the controller are known. One faces a decision of
choosing an appropriate identiﬁcation method to identify the unknown part of the system. Generally it is
either closed-loop or open-loop identiﬁcation. Whenever possible the preferred choice is the latter because
methodology for this approach is extensive. The former is more difﬁcult and fewer literature is available
about the topic.
Some of the methods used for system identiﬁcation in closed-loop are Urban Forssell (1999):
• Direct method: the feedback is ignored and the open loop system is identiﬁed using its inputs and
outputs.
• Indirect method: a transfer function of the closed loop system is identiﬁed from the extra-input or
setpoint to the controller and outputs from the system and afterwards the open-loop parameters are
determined using knowledge of the linear controller.
• Joint input-output method: inputs and outputs are jointly regarded as outputs of augmented system
driven by extra input or setpoint signal and noise. Later on the parameters of the open-loop system are
determined from the estimated system.
The signals mentioned are explained in the ﬁgure 3.1.
In the closed loop identiﬁcation, the prediction error method will estimate the system consistently under
assumptions that Ljung (1999):
25
==PAGE:48==
26
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
Figure 3.1: The block diagram of the closed loop system Urban Forssell (1999).
• the data set (input and output of the system) is informative enough meaning is able to distinguish
between different models in the model set, M ∗
• the model set contains the true system, S
The model estimate, ˆG will also have optimal accuracy.
Faulty inferences from closed-loop identiﬁcation may arise if Ljung (1999):
• the controller is too simple and hence destroys properties of the input signal like persistent excitation,
making the data not informative enough
• for output error models if the additive noise is not white it will not yield a consistent estimate of G
• subspace method will not result in consistent estimate
There are some situations where open-loop identiﬁcation is practically infeasible due to safety reasons or
because the open-loop system is unstable. In the project this was due to a time deadline for commissioning
the building and reluctance to tamper with the system conﬁguration. Therefore the Direct Method was
chosen for the system identiﬁcation. For that method a Prediction Error Method (PEM) should be applied to
estimate the model. The Direct Method has the following advantages Ljung (1999):
• no knowledge of the controller nor the feedback is needed
• the usual methods for open-loop estimation can be used
• optimal estimation accuracy is obtained if the model structure encompasses the true process
• identiﬁcation of unstable systems is possible if the closed loop system is stable
This approach requires good noise models. A noise model which does not match the true noise characteristics
will introduce a bias to estimates of the process ˆG. Unlike for the open-loop data where output error models
with independently parametrized noise models, result in a simple and consistent but not of optimal accuracy
model, it is the opposite for the closed-loop data. One should start with higher order model and keep reducing
it using for example a balanced realization Ljung (1999).
1.2
Identiﬁcation signal
Before choosing a suitable identiﬁcation signal it is important to specify what are the signals of the
system and which of those are involved in the identiﬁcation process. The signals in the system are:
==PAGE:49==
1. EXPERIMENT DESIGN
27
1. Control inputs:
• temperature setpoints to thermostats Tset[◦C]
• heat control value [%]
2. Measured disturbances
• global solar irradiation [W/m2] denoted in the thesis as Ψs or PsiS
• ambient temperature Ta[◦C]
3. Unknown disturbances
• ventilation ﬂow rate [l/s]
• internal gains from appliances, humans, lights [W]
4. Measured outputs
• zone temperatures denoted as Tr in the grey-box modelling and Ti in the black-box modelling in
units of [◦C]
• mass ﬂow rate [kg/h], in the thesis denoted as mf or ˙m depending on the context. ˙m = dm
dt is
reserved only in continuous time models. mf can be used also in discrete-time models.
The inputs and outputs of a model to be identiﬁed are for the experimental data:
• Inputs: control signal to valves [%]
• Output: zone temperatures [◦C]
and for the operational data:
• Inputs: mass ﬂow rate [kg/h]
• Output: zone temperatures [◦C]
Various process excitation signals exist for the purpose of identiﬁcation for linear and nonlinear systems.
Binary signals for example can only be suitable for linear processes as they do not perturb input non-
linearities. Some of the identiﬁcation signals are:
1. Step signal
2. Random binary sequence
3. Pseudo-random binary sequence (PRBS)
4. Random muti-step sequence
5. Sinusoid
The RFHS will be approximated as linear system at ﬁrst. Due to its properties and wide-spread use for
estimation of building’s models the PRBS signal is chosen in the project.
The PRBS is a deterministic signal with white noise properties. Therefore it can be pre-designed and used
later in an identiﬁcation experiment. It has no correlation with external signals like weather disturbances. It
is only suitable for linear model.
==PAGE:50==
28
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
Depending on the kind of model one is interested in developing, there are different approaches to
conducting the identiﬁcation experiment. For instance if one wants to develop multi-zone model of an
apartment with each zone encompassing one or many rooms, the selected zones will have to have independent
excitation signal. That can be achieved by shifting the same PRBS signal for all the zones in time. Following
the logic a three zone model would require the same three PRBS signals shifted in time with respect to
one another by one third of the signal length Peder Bacher (2010). A one-zone model of an apartment on
the other hand requires the use of only one PRBS signal applied to all the heat generators, in this instance
control valves in the radiant ﬂoor heating system.
The identiﬁcation signal needs to be persistently exciting to generate data set which is informative
enough Ljung (1999). In practice it means that the signal needs to excite the system in a frequency range at
which the dominant dynamics lays. A good estimation of the dynamic of the system is by conducting a step
response which can reveal its time constants. In general one wants to estimate slow and fast dynamics of an
apartment which can be modelled as second-order system.
The MATLAB function IDINPUT is used to generate PRBS signal for all the control valves. The
command is taking the length of the signal, M and the frequency band of the signal, B. The signal is allowed
to switch states at discrete times k = 0, λ, 2λ, . . . ,a.s.o, with the clock period, λ = 1
B being the shortest
interval the signal must stay in either of the states. In the thesis Thavlov (2008) it is presented that the PRBS
signal can be shaped to excite both small and large time constants by choosing λ and the order of PRBS
signal, n appropriately. The relationship between M and n must be established in order to correctly generate
the desired PRBS signal. The PRBS signal is periodic with period N = 2n −1 which is also its maximum
sequence length. The period of extended PRBS signal accounting for the shortest interval, λ is T = λ · N.
The easiest way to design a PRBS signal with the desired properties is as follows:
1. The choice of λ directly translates to the lower time constant.
2. The upper time constant one want to excite in the system is calculated from n using N = 2n −1 and
returning M = N.
The other way around, which is ﬁrst specifying the desired signal length, M and afterwards calculating
n is much more complicated and the detailed explanation of the algorithm is presented in MATLAB
documentation on IDINPUT command.
An example of the PRBS signal with λ = 3samples and n = 7 is presented in the ﬁgure 3.2 . The
signal can be scaled in time depending on the selected sample rate.
The following parameters were chosen for PRBS signal used for identiﬁcation based on the results from
the step response experiment 2.
• sample time: Ts = 60 sec
• clock period: λ = Tclk = 1/B = 20samples = 20min deﬁning the shortest period in the binary
sequence where the signal is either ON or OFF
• signal length: M = 12 · 60samples = 720samples = 12hours
• levels ∈{0, 1} are the values the signal can assume
These arguments for the IDINPUT function resulted in generated PRBS of extended period of 1260 samples,
which is given in the MATLAB warning message: "Warning: The PRBS signal delivered is the 720 ﬁrst
values of a full sequence of length 1260." and hence the order of the signal is n = log2(1260 · B + 1) = 6.
Furthermore λ · n = 120samples = 2hours which deﬁnes the longest time interval in the binary sequence
where the signal stays in one state.

The chosen parameters resulted in strongly autocorrelated PRBS sequence. This was due to the small ratio
between the signal length and clock period. Also the clock period should not be much larger than one.
In Thavlov (2008) the chosen values were λ = 30 min and nλ = 50 hours. Those values were selected
for poorly insulated test facility of light construction. In the case of airtight and heavy mass buildings like the
one in this project the time constant are expected to be much different, especially the cooling time constant
should be much larger and the heating constant much shorter.
1.3
Practical limitations
A number of practical limitations in the project obstructed the realization of the identiﬁcation experiment.
Therefore a new data set was collected from the apartment and a direct method for identiﬁcation was decided.
Some of the most important issues encountered are listed:
• Lack of remote control of the KNX system from DTU.
By this it is understood that actuators in the RFHS which are connected to the KNX bus cannot receive
actuation commands from the DTU control center as per the moment of writing this thesis. Only
signals exchanged on the KNX bus in some of the apartments of the building are transmitted over
Internet and recorded on DTU warehouse server. A remote overwriting of those signals is not enabled
yet. The functionality is expected to be implemented by September 2017. That implied the necessity
of conducting the identiﬁcation experiment in the building manually.
• The prerequisite of controlling the control valves through the hysteretic temperature controllers
manually.
This by itself introduces some delay in execution of the identiﬁcation signal because the setpoints
cannot be set immediately. After few hours of following PRBS signal the air temperature in some of
the rooms like bathroom reached value higher then the upper setpoint limit. At this point it was no
longer possible to control that zone using the approach and not all the control statuses of the valves
were consistent with PRBS sequence.
• No weather station installed on the site with the necessery measurements of outdoor temperature,
wind direction, wind speed and solar irradiance.
This affects the parameter estimation greatly resulting in parameter estimates with higher uncertainty.
==PAGE:52==
30
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
Also the identiﬁed model will not contain a term for exchanging the heat with the outside. Ultimately
the resulting model will not be able to predict inside air temperature with reasonable accuracy.
• The experiment was interrupted by ongoing construction work.
Therefore outages in power supply are visible in the data. This can be observed in the ﬁgures depicting
time gaps in the data 1, 2 and 3. The time gaps make the parameter identiﬁcation process more
troublesome and result in higher parameter uncertainty.
• The supply water temperature is measured before the shunt loop and manual thermostatic valve. The
return temperature is measured in the outlet manifold.
This allows for calculation of the total heat transferred to the apartment. Multi-zone models of the
apartment might not be possible without estimators of forward and return water temperatures for the
individual zones.
As mentioned before due to the nature of practicalities the indoor climate of the apartment is constantly
controlled in the closed loop and it cannot be broken for the duration of the identiﬁcation experiment.
Fortunately outputs of the controller are measured as well and hence both inputs to and outputs of the
controlled system are available. Consequently a direct method closed-loop identiﬁcation can be conducted.
After commissioning the building for use no more experiments were executed. Nevertheless the
operational data could still be useful for identiﬁcation. The data comes from a system were the owners
are changing temperature setpoints according to their preferences. This may result in the system operating
around the same temperature setpoint were the only deviations from it are due to disturbances which are
counteracted by the action of controller. The access to the operational data was impeded due to the following:
• The analysis of the data after apartments have been commissioned to operation in early April conﬁrms
that the underﬂoor heating system was deactivated.
That necessitated the use of the winter data when the frequent loss of connection happened and internal
gains from workers cannot be taken into account because the presence detectors signal only a presence
of at least one person.
• Access to any data from the apartment was impeded due to the lack of an agreement pertaining to
conﬁdentiality of the data.
This introduced considerable delay into the project because a model of the system is the core part of a
predictive controller.
• It is suspected that the nature of the data from the apartment could only allow for deriving a heat load
model of the apartment and not a model for predicting indoor temperature variations.
It means that a model based on the data would only predict how much energy needs to be provided to
the apartment to sustain temperature at a setpoint as a function of measured disturbances. Obviously the
prediction will not match the actual demand perfectly because the apartment is subject to disturbances
which are not measured.
• Due to the owner’s thermal comfort preferences the setpoints are not allowed to vary considerably.
As a consequence the only data available are operational data where zone temperatures vary around
their setpoints. The setpoints only change sporadically by few degrees. It hence might be difﬁcult for
an identiﬁcation algorithm to capture thermal dynamic information of the system.
• The ventilation system in many cases overruled the RFHS in meeting the needed thermal comfort
level and even surpassed it leading to temperature overshoot.
==PAGE:53==
1. EXPERIMENT DESIGN
31
1.4
Recommendations for overcoming the limitations
The author proposed a few suggestions for overcoming some of the limitations mentioned:
• Delay introduced in setting thermostat setpoints.
To facilitate the experiment, the valves state can be controlled directly by omitting the thermostats. The
device in question is the valve drive actuator (VAA/S12.230.2.1) receiving signals from thermostats.
The aim is to send commands ON and OFF for the valves directly, emulating the signals from
thermostats. That can be done by switching the valve drive actuator to manual mode with the push
of a button on the device. In this mode the device will be ignoring the telegrams from thermostats,
which could disrupt the properties of the identiﬁcation signal one wants to pertain to. Thereupon
commanding the valves is a matter of pressing the corresponding buttons on the valve drive actuator.
The pattern of the buttons pressed adheres to PRBS signal designed. The issue with it is that present
conﬁguration of the device ensures switching back to KNX operation mode after 30 min. Once that
happens telegrams from thermostats are received and this will destroy the properties of the PRBS
signal by commanding some valves to do the opposite action than what is commanded by PRBS. To
execute the experiment as planed without tampering too much with the KNX conﬁguration, at least
disabling the switching from manual to auto mode needs to be done. It is a matter of selecting one
option from a drop-down menu in the valve drive actuator. In the Topology view 3.3 select "1.1.22 (the
valve drive actuator - VAA/S12)". Afterwards in the Manual operation tab, enable "manual operation"
and set "do not reset manual operation to KNX operation". Unfortunately this approach was rejected
in the project due to concern about the safety of wooden ﬂoor. In the future when the link between
DTU and Frihavns Tårnet will be established the same can be easily achieved by sending disabling
object to the valve drive actuator and eventually the PRBS sequence.
Figure 3.3: ETS5 conﬁguration of the valve drive actuator
• Missing weather data
Attempts have been made to obtain the measurements from the nearby weather stations on United
Nations and International School buildings without success. The accepted solution was to obtain the
measurements from the DTU Byg weather station in Lyngby instead. A small portable weather station
==PAGE:54==
32
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
is planed to be installed in the future.
• Not enough variability in the indoor temperature
In case where the temperature does not vary dynamically, the identiﬁcation algorithm cannot estimate
parameters like time constants of the ﬂoor and room temperatures. Without that knowledge it is neither
possible to predict the temperature as function of measured disturbances. One of the approaches to
modelling is establishing a link between the measured disturbances and the amount of heat provided
to maintain a constant temperature. It can be achieved by looking at how much the inputs to the
ﬂoor heating system vary as function of the disturbances. Some variations of the temperatures inside
comfort bounds in the apartment must be allowed in order to exploit other objectives like providing
services to District Heating Operator (DHO) or cost savings for the user. One of the usages of the
model could be to provide the predicted heat load proﬁles to the DHOs which then would optimise
the district heating facility operation to match the supply with the demand. An aggregator managing
a group of residential apartments or buildings could provide the accumulated heat load predictions
increasing the granularity of the regulation of the supply water temperature and water ﬂow rate by the
DHO.
• Lack of some measurement datapoints.
A solution to missing datapoints is to install temperature sensors to measure the return water temper-
atures before the each control valve to estimate the heat dissipated in the ﬂoor for individual zones.
This does not require much ﬁnancial spending because the temperature sensors like PT1000 are not
expensive, they can be integrated with the existing system easily and the access to outlet manifold
with the control valves is easy through the control cabinet.
1.5
Experiment execution and physical set-up
The physical set-up
The data were collected using a laptop with installed ETS5 software by physically connecting it to
the KNX network in the apartment through ABB’s USB interface. The data was logged using diagnosis
capabilities of ETS5. There is an option of saving the telegrams exchanged on the KNX bus either locally on
the computer or externally on USB stick. The saved .xml log ﬁles needed to be converted to .csv ﬁles which
could then be easily processed by appropriate function in MATLAB or R. The temperature setpoints were
being changed manually on thermostats. The idea is to turn the control valves ON and OFF according to
the shape of the pre-designed PRBS signal by setting setpoints above or below the measured temperature
values. This results in sending the desired heating control values by thermostats to the valve drive actuator
controlling the valves through the valve drive.
Wooden ﬂoor safeguards
Some precautions have been undertaken to safeguard the wooden ﬂoor panels in order not to exceed the
maximum admissible temperature of around 27◦C:
• The manual valve regulating the forward water temperature to the apartment, mounted in the control
cabinet, is set in position 3.5 to limit the temperature to 35◦C in the inlet manifold.
• Limits have been set in thermostatic controllers for the set-point temperature.
In places where wooden panels are installed the limits are −9◦C to 5◦C from the basic set-point of
21◦C. In bathrooms where there are tiles installed the admissible deviations are respectively ±9◦C
==PAGE:55==
2. DATA ANALYSIS
33
from the basic set-point. As a side note the solution does not guarantee that the admissible wooden
ﬂoor temperature will not be exceeded. Assuming that this is the only safeguard put in place, then in
the case where the indoor air temperature is being cooled down constantly, e.g. by having all windows
open on cold day, the desired limited indoor air set-point could not be reached yet the RFHS would
become fully activated. If the forward water temperature would not be regulated by the manual valve
then this operation would require maximum heating power leading to the ﬂoor damage.
2
Data analysis
Two data sets are considered in the thesis for identiﬁcation. The ﬁrst set is collected from three designed
experiments conducted in March 2017 in one of the apartments on the 10th ﬂoor. The second set is the
operational data where a selected apartment’s indoor climate was controlled by the owners. That apartment
is located on the 11th ﬂoor. The latter data set was downloaded from DTU data warehouse and sensitive
data conﬁdentiality agreement needed to be signed before granting access to it. The former was collected in
three experiments during owner’s absence and hence the data was not sensitive.
2.1
Signal conditioning
The R language version 3.4.0 Patched was chosen for processing of the raw data and visualization. The
conditioning was slightly different for the two data sets mentioned above.
Experimental data
A number of steps needed to be taken to display the temperature in the zones:
1. removing duplicates by time: if few datapoints were marked with the same time stamp, only the ﬁrst
one was kept
2. sorting according to time
3. resample with 1 min resolution and align with the time of weather measurement
4. remove outliers
The temperature data was resampled at predeﬁned time instance grid by using zero-order-hold approximation.
This is because of the temperature is logged upon a change or at regular time intervals 1.2. This is a sufﬁcient
approximation regarding also the time resolution. Within that time interval the temperature cannot change
considerably.
Operational data
For this data set the conditioning was the following:
1. removing duplicates by time
2. sorting data by time
3. resample the temperature setpoints and the control signal with 5 min resolution using a constant
method of interpolation and align with the time of weather measurements
4. resample the measured temperature and the weather data with 5 min resolution using a linear interpo-
lation method and align with the time of weather measurements
==PAGE:56==
34
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
5. remove outliers
The resampling now was decided to be 5 min because the temperature change is more noticeable compared
to 1 min data. It was accomplish using APPROXFUN from STATS package in R language.
A note: When conditioning the input to the model - mass ﬂow rate, it was forgotten to scale the volumetric
ﬂow rate in units of [m3/h] to mass ﬂow rate in units of [kg/sec]. The motivation for the scaling is that
mass ﬂow rate, is utilized at estimating grey-box model where the term:
˙Qfh = ˙mcw (Tsupp −Tret)
(3.1)
is approximating the amount of heat transferred to the ﬂoor ˙Qfh in W, ˙m - water mass ﬂow in kg/sec, cw -
water speciﬁc heat in J/kg◦C, Tsupp - supply water temperature in ◦C, Tret - return water temperature.
2.2
Experiment results
Results from three experiments are discussed in this section. The temperature variation in different zones
for the experiments together with time gaps resulting from the loss of connection with KNX bus is seen in
ﬁgures in the appendix 2. The location of temperature sensors corresponding to the group addresses in the
legend of those ﬁgures can be found in the appendix 2.
In the ﬁgure 1 it can be seen that when the RFHS is requested to operation the rise time constant is
around 2 hours. The temperature drops are result of opening the windows.
The PRBS experiment can be seen in details in the appendix 2. The idea is to develop a one-zone model
of the apartment hence every control valve should receive the same excitation signal. It can be observed in
the second subplot of every ﬁgure that the excitation signal was a PRBS signal. Due to limitation put on the
setpoints eventually, it became impossible to shape the control signal to valves by changing the setpoints.
Consequently not every valve received the same signal for the duration of the experiment and it is the main
reason why this modelling approach cannot work. The following are the reasons why the quality of the
experiment determined poor results of the estimated model as shall be seen in the next section:
1. The designed PRBS was too short compared to the clock time Tclk to have white noise properties
which means the signal is correlated.
2. The acceptable and safe to the wooden ﬂoor approach to changing the control signal to valves turn
out to be useful in a short run. The excitation signals received by the valves were the same only for a
relatively short time. The prerequisite to obtaining the one-zone model is that the inputs for all the
zones are the same.
3. The short duration of the experiment. Usually the data is gathered over a longer than one day period
of time. At least one week data was used in Peder Bacher (2010).
4. There has been varying human activity in the apartment during the execution of the experiment which
introduced unmeasured disturbances in the form of internal gains.
5. Windows were being open by the construction workers.
6. Time gaps in the data resulting from loss of connection as can be seen in the ﬁgures in the appendix 2.
2.3
System identiﬁcation inputs
To prepare the data for identiﬁcation the important thing is to decide what kind of model is desired and
what should the inputs and outputs of the model be. Ljung (1999). At this stage it has been decided to develop
==PAGE:57==
2. DATA ANALYSIS
35
a simple heat dynamic model which is a one-zone model. The input to the model is a control signal assuming
values from the set {0, 1} and the output is a single representative vector of indoor temperature. It is a
single-input single-output (SISO) model however the dimension of a measurement vector is 7. Consequently
it has to be reduced before being fed into an identiﬁcation algorithm. There are various methods of achieving
it, e.g. taking a simple average, weighted average, linear discriminant analysis, canonical correlation analysis
or Principle Component Analysis (PCA). Firstly let us consider two ways of reducing the temperature vector
dimension.
Principle Component Analysis
The PCA can result in a signal with lower variation than taking average according to Thavlov (2008).
The purpose of PCA is to summarize a data set with less characteristics. As an example a data can be
characterised by many features but some of them might be correlated and hence redundant. The PCA projects
a data set onto a new frame of reference constructed from so called Principle Component (PC)s. The ﬁrst
principle component represents the maximum variance or the spread of data. The principal components are
given by the eigenvectors ordered by eigenvalues in decreasing manner. Hence the 2nd PC represents the
second largest variance in the data, a.s.o. An important issue is the scaling of the data before performing the
analysis. The PCA is using covariance matrix which will be different for scaled and non-scaled data amoeba
(n.d.).
The scree plot shows which principle component account for which percentage of variability in the data
21. It is shown that 88% of variability is in the ﬁrst PC. In appendix 2 there are ﬁgures showing more details
on the PCA performed.
As a result the following temperature was constructed from the ﬁrst principle component and is seen in
the Figure 3.4.
Weighted average
Another approach to dimension reduction is taking an average of the zone temperatures. In the thesis
a weighted average was used with the weights being a percentage of zone areas relative to the area of the
apartment 33 2. The resulting average temperature can be seen in the ﬁgure 3.5.
The ﬁgure 3.6 presents both the average and PC1 temperatures. The average is lower in value and has
smaller variability than the PC1 temperature. For that reason it was chosen in the project.
Heating control value
The output of the one-zone model of apartment is the heat control value. Again here it is required to
represent collectively all of the 7 signals with a single one. It is accomplished by performing a logical OR
operation on those signals. It is worth noticing that the same differential pressure will be maintained by
circulating pump regardless of how many control valves are open. The ﬂow rate in individual zones affecting
the amount of heat passed to the zones is depended on the number of active valves.

3
Modelling of heat dynamics
3.1
Grey-box model
A set of simple one-zone-apartment models are developed in the next subsections. The models are
presented as Stochastic Differential Equations and estimated using CTSM-R. The CTSM-R is a package for
R language for Continuous Time Stochastic Modelling developed at Informatics and Mathematical Modelling
department at DTU. It provides an algorithm for identifying models provided a data set of inputs and outputs
of the model using Maximum Likelihood estimator. The package can deal with missing observations in the
provided data set because of the implementation of the dynamic Kalman ﬁlter. For more details the reader is
referred to Niels Rode Kristensen (2003b) and Niels Rode Kristensen (2003a).
==PAGE:60==
38
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION

mar 18 00:00

type
Average
PC1
Figure 3.6: Average and PC1 temperatures comparison.
Model selection procedure
The identiﬁcation procedure to be followed in the project is presented in ﬁgure 3.8. Firstly an experiment
needs to be designed and the data from it collected. Afterwards the model set is speciﬁed from which
the best one will be chosen. This part is relying on engineering knowledge to provide the best guess of
models’ structure. Also the ﬁtting criterion is selected. It will provide a numerical value enabling to compare
different models against each other. The selection rule could be for example Least Squares. The model is
then computed and validated. If it cannot pass this phase, it requires the experiment re-design because of the
data being not informative enough or the choice of different model set or the different selection criterion or
simply computation of more complex model in the model set. Otherwise the computed model is selected as
the simplest in the model space which ﬁts well to the data according to the ﬁtting criterion selected Ljung
(1999).

Many different complexity models were presented in Tahersima (2012). The one represented by the RC
network 3.9 is suitable for simulation model of the system. It is the full order model made up of 5 states
representing the indoor temperature Ti, building envelope Te, two layers of the ﬂoor Tf1, Tf2 and water
temperature Tw. It captures dependencies between the indoor climate and the ambient temperature, the solar
radiation and the power from district heating Qf. The PhD thesis Tahersima (2012) presents low complexity
models for controller synthesis as well. An example is the ﬁrst order transfer function model with time delay:

which shows the dependency between the hot water ﬂow qfh and the ﬂoor heating output Qfh. It is
mentioned that the parameters kfh and τfh depend on the supply water temperature and vary slightly as
function of it, which means they can be regarded as constants.

3.2
Black-box model
The black-box approach is utilized in situations were one does not cares about the physical meaning of
the model. The modelling approach relies entirely on input-output data provided and specifying general
model structure. Just like in the grey-box approach also here the identiﬁcation procedure is an iterative
process of estimating models having different structures and comparing them against each other. Usually
one should start with the simplest possible modelLjung (n.d.). The black-box models were estimated using
SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION toolbox of MATLAB.
Identiﬁcation data
As will be argued in the validation section 4.1 the data set from experiments is not of sufﬁcient length.
Therefore in this approach the identiﬁcation data set used is 18 days long and referred to as operational data
in the thesis.

Figure 3.10: Input and output data from the selected apartment nr. 35 on 11th ﬂoor from January. Units of
the signals from the top to the bottom plot are respectively [◦C], [m3/h], [W/m2] and [◦C]
Data characteristics
The identiﬁcation data was analysed by MATLAB’s ADVICE command. The following is the character-
istic of the data comprising of 5338 samples. According to the information provided by the function and
what can be seen in the ﬁgure 3.10 the inputs and output have none zero means and it is recommended to
remove them before estimation. There are three exceptions to the rule:
• The signals are measured relative to the system’s equilibrium.
• There is an integrator in the system and the levels of inputs and outputs is essential to identify the
integration
• A nonlinear AutoRegressive with eXogenous terms model (ARX) model is to be estimated from the
data
Based on the ﬁrst exception, the means are not removed from the signals.
The ﬂow rate is persistently exciting of order 50 meaning that models up to order 50 will be estimated
without any problems. Parameters associated with the input should be estimated with low uncertainty. The
==PAGE:66==
44
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
level of excitation for all the inputs is [50 50 50].
Moreover there has been detected a direct term in the data from input mf[k] to output yTr[k]. The
reasons for that might be due to two reasons:
• a feedback from output y[k] to input u[k] through a P-controller. It is recommended to remove its
effect from the model by setting delay time to nk > 0 for the input in state-space and input-output
models.
• there is a direct feed-through term in the system. In such a case nk = 0 is recommended for the
corresponding input in PEM.
The identiﬁcation data was gathered from the closed-loop system with the aim of using the direct method of
estimation. The hysteretic controller was enabled the entire time, hence the strong indication of the feedback
in the data and direct term from output yTr[k] to input mf[k] as mentioned above.
MATLAB advices to carefully interpret results of the frequency response being estimated by the function
SPA as well as of Output Error Model (OEM)s. In case of feedback in the data it is advised to estimate a
model with large disturbance component. As far as polynomial models are concerned Box-Jenkins Model
(BJ) should yield better accuracy results than OEM. State space models should be estimated together with
the disturbance model (the option ’DisturbanceModel’= ’Estimate’). N4SID method works better when the
weight is set to N4Weight = "SSARX" compared to other options.
Furthermore there is no sign of non-linearities in the given data set therefore a linear model will be
estimated.
Additionally the data was checked for time delays using the function DELAYEST. It estimated that there
is a time delay of nkmf = 10 samples from the mf input to the output and nkT a = 9 samples from Ta to
the output temperature yTr. No dead time was detected from solar irradiance Ψs meaning that its effect on
the indoor climate is immediate.
Model structure selection
The polynomial models are generalization of a transfer function expressing relationship between the
input u[k], the output y[k] and the white noise e[k]:
A(q)y[k] =
nu
X
i=1
Bi(q)
Fi(q) ui[k −nk,i] + C(q)
D(q)e[k]
(3.9)
They are expressed in terms of polynomials A, B, C, D, F. Depending on the structure they model the
dynamics and noise properties to a varying level of ﬂexibility. The application determines whether the
noise and dynamics should have common poles determined by the poles of A(q) polynomial or not. When
disturbances enter into the input, it is beneﬁcial to have common poles. The poles of Fi are unique to system
dynamics and poles of D are unique to disturbance model Ljung (n.d.).
The System Identiﬁcation toolbox allows for various model structures. The linear models identiﬁed in
this project were:
• Polynomial models
1. ARX described by the equation
A(q)y[k] = B(q)u[k −nk] + e[k],
(3.10)
where A = 1+a1q−1+. . .+anaq−na polynomial forms the autoregressive part,B = b1q−1−nk+
. . . + b−nb−nk
nb
polynomial and e is noise with Gaussian distribution.
The model does not allow for independent noise and dynamics models.
==PAGE:67==
3. MODELLING OF HEAT DYNAMICS
45
2. AutoRegressive Moving Average with eXogenous terms model (ARMAX) described by the
equation
A(q)y[k] = B(q)u[k −nk] + C(q)e[k],
(3.11)
where the polynomial C = 1 + c1q−1 + . . . + cncq−nc models the noise.
The model is useful when dominating disturbances enter at the input. It allows for ﬂexible noise
model.
3. OEM described by
y[k] = B(q)
F(q)u[k −nk] + e[k]
(3.12)
It is preferred when no noise model is needed.
4. BJ represented as:
y[k] = B(q)
F(q)u[k −nk] + e[k]
(3.13)
The model should be chosen when the noise is mainly a measurement noise and not a one which
enters at the input. It models noise and dynamics independently.
• State space model
The structure selection may depend on the application the model will be used in or simply on preferences
Ljung (n.d.).
Order selection
Dependent on the type of model, the order have different meaning. For state space models, it denotes
the number of states whereas for polynomial models it means the number of coefﬁcients. The System
Identiﬁcation Toolbox allows for estimating the model order directly from the data. One should be careful
when selecting the order because for the same model structure higher order will enable the model to
encompass complex phenomena but at the cost of decreasing its reliability Ljung (n.d.).
Type of estimation
The ARMAX, OEM,BJ models are estimated using PEM where given a model M(θ) which depends on
the parameter set θ ∈DM and the data set ZN = [y(1), u(1), y(2), u(2), . . . , y(N), u(N)] the parameter
vector ˆ
θN is found by solving the minimization problem:
ˆ
θN(ZN) = arg min
θ∈DM
VN(θ, ZN)
(3.14)
with the norm
VN(θ, ZN) = 1
N
N
X
k=1
L(ϵF (k, θ)),
(3.15)
where L(·) is a scalar-valued function and ϵF is the ﬁltered prediction error sequence ϵ through a linear and
stable ﬁlter. The prediction error sequence is :
ϵ(k, θ) = y(k) −ˆy(k|θ),
∀k = 1, 2, . . . , N
(3.16)
The choice of L(·) is often a quadratic norm:
L(ϵ) = 1

(3.17)
==PAGE:68==
46
CHAPTER 3. MODELLING AND SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION
For more information about the method the reader is referred to Ljung (1999).
The ARX model can be estimated using either Least-Squares or Instrumental Variables (IV) method.
The Least-Square method is a special case of PEM and it minimizes the sum of squares of the expression
(b1u[k −nk] + . . . + bnbu[k −nk −nb + 1]) −(y[k] + a1y[k −1] + . . . + anay[k −na]) with respect to
coefﬁcients in A and B. It uses QR-factorization for overdetermined linear equations Ljung (n.d.). The
IV method on the other hand makes the expression uncorrelated with certain linear combinations of the
inputs when searching for the optimal polynomial’s coefﬁcients in A and B from the Equation 3.10. The two
methods are different with respect to how they regard noise. The Least-Squares method assumes white noise
and IV method should be used whenever the noise in the system is not white Ljung (n.d.). More information
can be found in Ljung (1999).
Criteria for model validation
There are a few measures of model quality. The criterion used in the project is Akaike’s Final Prediction
Error (FPE). It provides a way of comparing different models between themselves. The model with the
highest accuracy has the lowest value of FPE. The criterion is deﬁned as
FPE = V (1 + 2d/N) ,
(3.18)
where V is the loss function, d is the parameter number and N is the number of values in the estimation data
set. It can be see that high number of parameters is penalized. The loss function is deﬁned as Ljung (n.d.):
V = det
 
1
N
N
X
k=1
ϵ(k, θN)ϵ(k, θN)T
!
(3.19)
Another criterion called Mean Square Error (MSE) is deﬁned as the sum of systematic error (bias) and
random error (variance) Ljung (n.d.):
MSE = |bias|2 + variance
(3.20)
Typically high order model yields small systematic error but large random error.
3.3
Thermostat model
In the section a thermostat model will be derived. There are various methods of modelling hysteresis
in the literature. Examples involve Bouc–Wen, ﬁnite automata, Preisach, Prandtl–Ishlinskii, Maxwell-
Slip models. The one focused on in the thesis, draws from the bifurcation theory. It will be shown for
demonstration purposes nevertheless it was decided to replace the physical thermostat model with a PID
controller to maintain the linearity of the combined model and design a linear MPC. For future work it is
recommended to try with a nonlinear model.
Bifurcation model of hysteresis
Hysteresis effect can be created by making use of the bifurcation theory. Having a ﬁrst order ODE
depended on a parameter µ:
˙x = f(x) = µ + x −x3,
(3.21)
as µ is varied, the stationary solution, x changes accordingly. One can draw a stability curve (x, µ) and see
that even though from the equation 3.21, the curve will be in S like shape, the stable solutions will assume
values on the edges of the curve and jump to the opposite edge when surpassing the peaks 3.11. On the curve
==PAGE:69==
3. MODELLING OF HEAT DYNAMICS
47
Figure 3.11: The (x, µ) curve and the hysteresis effect. From (n.d.).
it is possible to mark stable and unstable node’s proﬁles. In reality a stable solution will be always taken.
The jumps to another stable solution on another proﬁle happen at bifurcation points. They are calculated as
follows:
1. First ﬁnd the stationary points:
Set the derivative to zero in the equation 3.21 and solve for µ:
˙x = 0 ⇔µ = −x + x3
(3.22)
2. Calculate the derivative w.r.t. x and solve it for x:
df

The bifurcation points desired are µb = ±0.5 because the thermostats employ a hysteretic control function
with hysteresis width of 1◦C. This can be achieved by adding another degree of freedom to 3.21 - parameter
r:
˙x = f(x) = µ + rx −x3
(3.25)
From (n.d.) it is claimed that for r > 0 the bifurcation points can be calculated as µb(r) = ±2(r/3)3/2.
One has ±0.5 = ±2(r/3)3/2 ⇔r = 3(0.5)4/3. Using this value a simulation is conducted 3.12 and it can
be seen that the hysteresis effect is achieved.
PID controller
In the project instead of hysteretic controller a linear PID is used like in the thesis Tahersima (2012).
The starting point for the controller design was that the closed-loop response time should be about 6000
sec which is 1 hour and 40 min. Afterwards it was tuned to have little overshoot and faster response time.
The resulting controller is a discrete-time PIDF controller in parallel form:
