4
Model validation
The model validation step is important because it provides information on accuracy of an estimated
model. If the model meets accuracy criteria it can be used for example for prediction purposes. The standard
method of validation is a whitness test of residuals. The residuals are computed as time series of the
differences between the measurements and the one step ahead predictions of them:
rk = yk −ˆyk|k−1,
∀k
(3.27)
In the following subsection grey-box models will be estimated and validated. In this methodology the
model uncertainties are modelled as stochastic variables. If the residuals do not show white noise properties,
it means that some dynamics have been neglected in the model. The test can be performed by checking
==PAGE:71==
4. MODEL VALIDATION
49
Auto-Correlation Function (ACF) of residuals and its cumulative periodogram. The latter is interpreted in
terms of Fourier analysis which underlying assumption is that any signal can be decomposed into sinusoids
of different frequencies. The same is done for residuals. If they have white noise properties, their frequency
spectrum will be ﬂat because every frequency will have the same amplitude. Therefore its cumulative
periodogram will be a straight line.
4.1
Grey-box models
Each parameter in the CTSM-R requires a boundary range and initial guess of the solution. The better
the guess the less iterations are needed for the algorithm to converge. The boundaries are set wide to explore
the solution space especially for thermal capacitances. The range is several orders of magnitude. Whenever
possible parameters were ﬁxed to known values so that the estimated parameters of the model pertain to
physical values as much as possible. All the models presented are estimated on a data set from the PRBS
experiment. Similar results were obtained using data from two other experiments as well as operational
data with the heat control value as input to the model instead of water ﬂow rate. It is important to note
that the models were estimated assuming there is no time delay in the system. CTSM-R does not allow
to estimate time delays therefore if there is a delay in the system, the data set fed into the identiﬁcation
algorithm must reﬂect that delay. Similar results were obtained by delaying output vector compared to the
input using ﬁndings from data set inspection explained in the subsection 2.2.

Some of the parameters were ﬁxed during estimation. Those are the effective area of windows Aw =

Looking at the standard deviance of the parameters the values are only one or two orders lower compared
to the estimates. Examples are Ca, Cf, k, thermal resistances Rf, Rr, Rs, Rv. That means the estimates are
very uncertain.
The column Pr(> |t|) being less than 0.05 for all the parameters means that the model is not over-
parametrized. A value above 0.05 would mean that the corresponding initial state of parameter is insigniﬁcant
and effectively equal to 0. The next column of the table is showing value which are not close to zero meaning
that the solution found is not the true optimum. That is especially apparent for the initial states Tf0 and
Tr0, thermal capacity of the ﬂoor Cf. Last column leads to a conclusion that because none of the values are
signiﬁcant compared to dF/dPar value in the corresponding row of the table, none of the parameters are
close to their set boundaries.

TfTf2Tr model
Next the TfTr model is extended by another state modelling thermodynamics of the second layer of the

PRBS experiment and later on validated.
The ﬁgure 3.22 shows that the extended model yielded worse results. The model does not capture the
dynamics of the system well. By analysing the ﬁgure 3.19 one can see that there is a big spike in residuals at
the beginning and it affects the results. An explanation for this spike occurrence has not been found. The
model errors lay in the low frequency range. The autocorrelation function 3.20 conﬁrms that the biggest
correlation is noted at the beginning. In the raw periodogram of residuals plot 3.21 it is seen that after
the initial spike the spectral density of residuals is showing that the signal is made up of sinusoids which
frequencies are integer multiple of a dominant one and similar amplitude.
TeTfTrQfh model
The last model to estimate and validate is the linear state space model with 4 states, 1 output and 3 inputs.
This is the only linear model of out the three proposed. It is focusing on modelling dynamics of the building
envelope, ﬂoor and room temperature and the heat transfer from water to the ﬂoor. During estimation it was
assumed that there is no time delay, τd = 0 in 3.8.

Looking at the cumulative periodogram 3.24 it can be seen that the linear model is sufﬁcient enough to
capture the dynamics of the system with exception of couple of frequency ranges. A raw periodogram can
be seen in 3.25 which resembles white noise spectrum.
The autocorrelation function 3.26 shows slight correlation of the residuals which can be seen in the
ﬁgure 3.23.
The table 3.5 shows the estimates of the parameters and their signiﬁcance. The ˆCa estimate has low
uncertainty. Estimates of Ce and Cf have standard deviations only one order of magnitude lower than
their estimates so tey are quite uncertain. UAe and UAf estimates are low in uncertainty. The 4th column
indicates that the model may be over-parametrized with the three of the four process noise standard deviations
having the fraction of the probability of the corresponding t-distribution above 0.05. That infomration can
be discarded because the noise is integral part of the stochastic differential equations. The derivative of
the objective function (deﬁned in Niels Rode Kristensen (2003a)) w.r.t. parameters is not zero meaning
the found solution is not a minimum. The penalty function (deﬁned in Niels Rode Kristensen (2003a))
w.r.t. parameters is not signiﬁcant compared to the derivative of the objective function and hence the found
solution is not close to the parameter boundaries. Making the boundaries wider will most likely not affect
the solution because the search algorithm was not searching for the solution close to parameter boundaries.
Lastly the table 3.6 shows little correlation between the parameters. Summarizing the linear model seems to
be the best of the three analysed so far and has the advantage of obeying the superposition rule.

Validation using MATLAB and SIMULINK
For the reason mentioned above the selected model is TeTfTrQfh. Before using the model for Model
Predictive Control it is important to test how well it can predict the indoor temperature. For that purpose
simulated response of the model needs to checked. MATLAB software, version 2015B and SIMULINK
version 8.6 were chosen as testing environment. It was decided to test ODEs instead of SDEs by neglecting
the stochastic terms. The set of linear ODE equations are simulated using a SIMULINK block called
"Interpreted MATLAB Fcn" which calls a MATLAB function where the derivatives are deﬁned. The
derivatives are integrated using ﬁxed step size equal to 1 sec and numerical solver ode4 (Runge-Kutta).
In the ﬁgure 3.27 it can be observed that the solar irradiance Ψs has the biggest inﬂuence on the indoor
temperature Tr. Two rises of Tr are observed when solar radiation is non-zero. There seems to be no visual
effect from the ambient temperature which for demonstration purposes is a sinusoid with a period of one
day and amplitude of 3◦C. The most important is that the control input, ﬂow status is not changing the
indoor climate. That means that the model is uncontrollable from that input and hence cannot be used in
MPC where the controller tries to steer the indoor temperature by shaping the ﬂow signal. To conﬁrm the
above observation one can look at the ﬁgure 3.28 with model states. The last plot of envelope temperature
shows no effect from sinusoidal ambient temperature, Ta. Looking at the state Qfh one can see a decaying
exponential from initial value Qfh0 which was estimated by CTSM-R. There is no effect of ﬂow status
signal on the state.

Summarizing the poor results of the estimation stems from a few reasons:
1. Too short experiment duration
2. Missing data being result of connectivity losses
3. Complexity of the system under consideration
4. Not adequate input because of the mass ﬂow rate being unavailable during the identiﬁcation experiment
on the 10th ﬂoor.
5. Mistakenly using the heat control value instead of water ﬂow rate when estimating grey-box models
on operational data set.
6. Forgotten to scale volumetric ﬂow rate - ˙V [m3/h] to mass ﬂow rate - ˙m[kg/sec].
7. Not synchronised excitation signals for all the control valves during the experiment
8. Neglecting time delays in the system
In the next section the above mentioned will be taken into account. Especially when a complex system
is considered like the one dealt with, the preferred estimation technique is the black-box approach. It has
the drawback of being not physically interpretable unlike the grey-box approach which is based on the

combination of ﬁrst principle mathematical equations and the data. In grey-box models the states have a
physical meaning. The advantage of the black-box is that it is simple and can be successfully applied in
control applications whereas the grey-box often contains many states and could be nonlinear which is good
for predictions but requires model reduction and linearization when applied in control for fast computations.
It should be mentioned that this phase of validation exposed the poor accuracy of the black-box model
estimated on operational data as well.
4.2
Black-box
As observed during validation, the selected grey-box model omits important information about the system.
For example the logical OR operation used to calculate the input to the system, ﬁlters out information about
the number of active valves. It only answers the question: is any of the valves open? This is important
because the more valves are active the bigger the radiation surface and more energy is transferred to the
room.
In the thesis the mass ﬂow rate measurements occurring for example in equation 3.2 were unavailable
during the experiment and had to be replaced by the heat control value in the range 0 to 100%. This
contributed to poor estimation results. In the black box the volumetric ﬂow rate is decided as input signal to
the model and a new data set is used. The recorded values of the ﬂow rate to the apartment 3.7 can be used
as a starting point to estimating the ﬂow in the one selected room 2.4. The signal is then used in the logical

As mentioned earlier the one-zone model approach is not justiﬁed in situations where not all the excitation
signals are synchronised. Therefore the focus will be shifted to modelling of one room. A simpliﬁcation in
this case will be to neglect the heat exchange between adjacent rooms.
To identify the new model the operational data of one-month duration is decided to be used. The
volumetric ﬂow rate depicted in the Figure 3.7 is used as input to the model. The output is seen in the Figure
3.30.
Predicted output vs simulated output
The primal use of the model estimated in the thesis is to simulate its output. It is important to distinguish
between predicted and simulated output. The prediction means forecasting the model’s output over the
prediction horizon using the measured past and current input and output. The simulation uses only the initial
states and input sequence to forecast the model output over simulation horizon. The latter is a better way of
validating the model. The difference between the two is that in prediction the past outputs are measured
whereas in simulation the values are computed.

Cross-validation
The data set was divided into estimation and validation data sets in proportion 7 : 3. The idea is to
estimate a model of the estimation data and test it accuracy of prediction on validation/test data. Both data
sets should be independent. This process is called cross-validation and prevents over-ﬁtting. It test how the
estimated model performs by being generalized on another data set. The estimation data 24 was taken from
the end period of the experiment were the thermal comfort was sustained by solar irradiance alone for 4 days
to capture the effect of both the water ﬂow and the solar irradiance on the system. The validation data can be
seen in the ﬁgure 25.
Residual analysis
The part of the data set which a selected model cannot replicate is called residuals 3.16. They are
computed on the validation data set. Two tests that the residuals should pass are Ljung (1999):
• Whitness test indicating that the residuals are independent and hence the model captures well the
dynamic of the system. In other words the error made is due to variance and not systematic error.
• Independence between residuals and past inputs. This provides valuable information about the model
structure. The test can reveal time delay in the system. If the delay is equal to 2 samples there will be

The estimated models
The table 3.7 presents the estimation results. Even though the FPE is the lowest for model arx519 the
simulated output cannot follow the measurements very well which can be seen in 3.29. The criterion can be
used to compare only different models of the same structure.
The ﬁt to estimation data can be a deceiving factor because very well ﬁt may mean over-ﬁtting. What
should be tested is how well a model is reproducing a new data set, for example a validation data.
It is noted that BJ model has a very bad MSE and from the fact for which purposes it is used, it can be
concluded that the system’s main noise source is not only the measurement noise. The best performing out
of the investigated models is ss4 and it will be selected for further analysis.
The models investigated have the following structure:
1. arx3110: na=3 nb=[1 1 1] nk=[10 0 9]
2. arx519: na=5 nb=[1 1 1] nk=[9 9 9]
3. oe1210: nb=[1 1 1] nf=[2 2 2] nk=[10 0 9]
4. oe2210: nb=[2 2 2] nf=[2 2 2] nk=[10 0 9]

CHAPTER 4
Economic Model Predictive Control
Model Predictive Control is a control methodology which is mainly applied in process industry, most
commonly in petrochemical sector. Maciejowski (10 October 2000) Its advantages compared to traditional
wide-spread methodology - Proportional-Integral-Derivative (PID) control are to name a few Maciejowski
(10 October 2000) and Jørgensen (2016):
• Handling of complex dynamical systems involving time delays, inverse response, nonlinearity, cross-
coupling in a multivariate systems. That is attributed to the the fact that MPC makes use of a model of
process.
• Inclusion of feedback from measurements, feedforward of references and measured disturbances in
the problem of optimizing predicted plant output changes.
• Accommodating actuator and output constraints.
• Possibility of handling missing measurements which is made possible via dynamical state observer
with time-varying matrices of the model of the plant.
• Closer to constraints operation leading to economical beneﬁts
The principle of operation of MPC is easy to comprehend. It is based on a so called receding-horizon strategy
where at each time step of the discrete-time controller a prediction of the plant’s outputs is made based off of
a model for a ﬁxed time window, N steps ahead. Each step of the controller is equal to the data sampling
time Ts. At a new step, a new prediction is made into the future. Effectively the prediction window is
moving with the time hence the name - receeding horizon strategy.
The MPC as its name indicates is based on a model of a plant. The accuracy of output predictions
strongly depends on the model used for that purpose. In other words ideally one aims at obtaining a model
closely resembling behaviour of the plant in a frequency spectrum of interest. For example if a controller is
expected to work in a lower frequency range then one allows for higher model-plant mismatch errors for high
frequencies. Therefore a great importance should be placed on model’s resemblance of the plant behaviour
in a frequency spectrum, the controller is expected to operate. The accuracy of an estimated model must be
assured by a control engineer. The degree of it depends on the application and requirements of customers.
They may want to regulate the indoor temperature within a certain level of comfort bounds. Consequently
the MPC has to be making forecasts of the temperature with the corresponding level of accuracy not to
violate these comfort bounds. Therefore usually requirements are determined by customers and it is up to a
control engineer to meet those. In case of traditional controllers the tunning of them is based on a model
of the plant whereas MPC directly makes use of it in an on-line computation of optimized control actions.
Hence the requirements for precision of the control derive from the model. One of the advantages mentioned
71
==PAGE:94==
72
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
is that any model-plant mismatch errors can be corrected by feedback from measurements taken at each time
step. In case of a poor model the predictions will be more uncertain the further into the future they are made.
In fact it is impossible to perfectly capture the behaviour of any plant, which is non-linear in its nature to a
varying degree. Therefore those modelling errors are inevitable. MPC methodology inherently accounts
for the adversity by making educated guess of the future evolution of the outputs of the plant using all the
available knowledge of forecasted disturbaces to do so. It is then adjusting the control actions causing the
output trajectory to reach the reference speciﬁed and trying to meet certain objectives deﬁned in the loss
function to be optimized. Only the ﬁrst control action is implemented on the plant before moving with the
prediction horizon and taking measurements from the plant to adjust its information about the plant.
When making predictions of the output which are a function of control input sequence, the controller
is using a user-deﬁned numerical metric or cost function to asses to which degree a goal is achieved when
determining the optimal control action sequence. It is also a way of comparing different sequences of action
against each other to select the optimal one in some sense - there are different norms one can choose between.
The optimality is determined by the form of the objective function. It could be in a least squares sense if the
objective to be minimized or maximized is Euclidean norm of differences between a reference and measured
quantity. In other words it is sum of squares of the differences. Another option would be L1 norm which is
deﬁned as absolute deviation. Others norms are: weighted least squares or compound loss function made up
of several terms usually linear or quadratic Maciejowski (10 October 2000).
It is worth to point out that the cost function is built from the estimates of output evolution and not
the actual output of the plant. Needless to say measurements not always correspond to outputs and if
they do, they are corrupted by measurement noise which stems from inherent imperfections of sensors. A
consequence of it is that any constraint deﬁned in the MPC problem will be validated or put into context of
the predicted output. Therefore even though the real output which could be approximated by measurement
can be relatively far away from violating a constraint at a given moment, the predicted by the model output
will exceed that constraint after some steps ahead and if it is a hard constraint then optimization might fail.
That can be inevitable if all the tried control actions within input bounds lead to the hard constraint violation
leaving no room for correction or escaping route from the situation. There is a concept of soft constraint
problem where this issue is alleviated by adding a slack variable in the constraints and objective function.
Basically it means that the constraints can be violated if such a situation cannot be avoided but it will be
penalised in the objective function according to a degree of the violation. The algorithm will try to avoid this
when possible since the cost function is tried to be minimized Jørgensen (2017). This objective function is
usually in a quadratic form but in a case of EMPC is often linear function of control variables.
The type of MPC focused on in this dissertation is EMPC. The main difference between traditional MPC
and EMPC is that in the latter an objective function contains a term directly related to operational costs and
a reference tracking term is omitted. For traditional MPC tracking control, the objective is to minimize the
error between a reference trajectory and the measured output to maintain the process variables around a
desired steady state. The EMPC on the other hand optimizes the process operations in a time-varying fashion
with the objective of minimizing the cost. Here the process operates only in the transient state.
In this chapter EMPC will be formulated for a system with direct feed-through term from input to output
which requires different from traditional approach. It is discussed in details in the chapter.
1
Simulation setup
A Model Predictive Controller is usually tested in a simulation before being deployed on a real plant.
There is also an option of a so called hardware-in-the-loop simulation where an MPC controller being
==PAGE:95==
2. PROBLEM FORMULATION
73
evaluated is running in a simulation environment, e.g. on a real-time PC or embedded system and interacts
with a physical system through I/O expansion card. That way the control signal is directly actuating the
system and measured signals are fed back into the algorithm. Therefore the complexity of the system is
brought into the problem. It allows for validating the accuracy level of a system’s model used in the controller.
Since any model is merely an approximation of a system it inevitably imposes modelling errors which affect
performance and robustness of the controller on a real system. In the scope of the project an approach taken
is the software-in-the-loop simulation, where the ’actual’ plant is mathematically represented. It is assumed
that there is no mismatch between the model and the plant. It was decided so due to the inability to estimate
a simple, reasonably accurate model used for predictions, not to mention more complex simulation model
whose task would be to emulate a real plant. The estimated discrete-time linear time-invariant model is
presented here again for the reader’s convenience:

2
Problem formulation
Model Predictive Control can be presented in the following graphical form 4.1. The notation presented
on the diagram 4.1 is consistent with the notation used in the thesis with the following exceptions in the
thesis:
• the measured disturbances are denoted as dk
• the input disturbance model is G
• the unmeasured input disturbances are denoted as wk and are a gaussian distributed white noise
• the white measurement noise denoted as vk and there is no measurement noise model
• output disturbance model is Gy
As is seen on the diagram 4.1 the core part of the MPC system constitutes of the plant model which can
be reﬁned by adding input, output disturbance and measurement noise models. The models act like ﬁlters for
==PAGE:96==
74
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
Figure 4.1:
Model of an MPC system.
Courtesy of https://se.mathworks.com/help/mpc/ug/
controller-state-estimation.html
white noise on their inputs and change the properties of the noise on their output to have characteristics of
disturbances being observed acting on the real plant and a purpose of the measurement noise model is to add
the same amount of uncertainty to the simulated model’s output as speciﬁed in data sheet of sensors being
used on the plant. In other words it accounts for the uncertainty of sensors therefore revealing information
about the true output.
2.1
Hierarchical control system
Buildings are examples of complex multi-input, multi-output systems with exogenous disturbances
acting on it. An examples of disturbances are ambient temperature, solar irradiation, internal gains from
humans, household appliances. Some of the actors are depicted in the ﬁgure 4.2. The indoor climate control
is challenging and strongly depended on the adequate control structure Tahersima (2012). In the framework
of the dissertation an approach taken is similar to the one presented in Tahersima (2012) and S Hwan Cho
(2012). A hierarchy control structure for the indoor climate regulation is adopted where a supervisory
controller - the MPC is optimizing temperature setpoints for the lower level local controllers - the zone
thermostats. One of the purpose of such a structure as opposed to a user adjusting temperature setpoints
on their own is providing ﬂexibility to the District Heating Operator (DHO). The MPC can schedule the
setpoints optimally with regards to the heat price so that peak shaving can be achieved, reducing the burden
on DHO to supply demanded heat at peak hours. This automates the task for the user. They can still override
the decision of supervisory controller but they are given ﬁnancial incentive to use it. By giving up few
degrees of Celsius of thermal comfort potential savings can be achieved. Since the dynamical price is
correlated with the heat demand and production, the user decision to follow the optimization trajectory is not
only ﬁnancially beneﬁcial for them but for the DHO as well.
A distribution control system is used where the control responsibility is redistributed throughout the
system. It increases reliability and stability of the overall system Tahersima (2012). In the project local
thermostats are responsible for temperature regulation or in other words maintaining a requested setpoint.
The MPC controller is responsible for providing services to the DHO by optimizing those setpoints as a
==PAGE:97==
2. PROBLEM FORMULATION
75
function of the heat price.
The ﬁgure 4.3 presents the distributed control architecture where Building Energy Management System
(BEMS) is connected with the Grid Energy Management System (Grid EMS) but in principle it well could
be DHO Energy Management System (EMS). The Grid EMS is providing the EMPC with a price signal and
enable signal. MPC also uses weather forecasts, local Renewable Energy Generators (REG) if the building
have access to it and consumption prediction. In return the MPC reports back on which decision it is taking.
The MPC is responsible for issuing commands for local controllers which in this ﬁgure are PI controllers but
in the project they are hysteretic controllers. The building is devided into several zones which climate is
controlled by independent local controllers. The zone temperatures are fed back into MPC.

The bottom ones represent the factors having direct effect on the indoor temperature. Some of them like the
heating and internal gains are to some extend controlled by the MPC.
In the ﬁgure 4.2 there elements which can be controlled like the heating by thermostats, internal gains
from appliances by scheduling their operation in time. There is also ventilation which is controlled centrally
by an independent control unit.
2.2
MPC components
MPC controller can be decomposed into several components:
• State estimator - ﬁltering state and unmeasured disturbance estimates- ˆxk|k, ˆwk|k
• Output predictor - predicting the sequence of future outputs ˆZk based on the ﬁltered state and
disturbance estimates.An explicit prediction equation is required if a minimized quantity depends on
the output.
• Optimizer/Regulator - calculating optimal control sequence U ∗
k minimizing an objective function
according to some norm by solving an optimization problem.
Each of those components are described in the following subsections in details. The MPC is designed for the
deterministic part of the model 4.3 so that the model can be written in the form:

In this form, suited for a state observer, the model does not have a perfectly correlated process and
measurement noise any more.
Nonetheless it is easy to formulate MPC problem to be suited for models in innovation form where the
noises are correlated as well J. B. Jørgensen (2011).
Firstly the time delays in the inputs and an internal delay of 10 samples in the model 4.3 need to be
taken care of as they are not brought into consideration when designing Kalman ﬁlter. It has been veriﬁed in
simulation that by not absorbing delays into system dynamics the state observer gets unstable because the
Kalman gain matrix gets ill-conditioned. Also the MATLAB documentation warns against such internal
delays which are not supported by the KALMAN command. A way of dealing with it is to convert time
delays into extra states in a 1-to-1 manner so that each delay will correspond to one extra state in the
transformed model. This mechanism is utilised in a MATLAB function - textitabsorbDelay which places
poles at z = 0. It is shown how to accomplish it in appendix 2. Basically, ﬁrstly all the time delays need to
be integrated into system dynamics of the estimated model of RFHS. Afterwards the model is used to close
the loop with a PID controller to form the ﬁnal system. Closing the loop is detailed in appendix 2.
The ﬁnal thing is to get rid of uncontrollable and unobservable states by taking a minimal realisation of
the model. The resulting model with 9 both unobservable and uncontrollable states removed has a minimal
==PAGE:99==
2. PROBLEM FORMULATION
77
order and same magnitude response as the original model 4.4. The only difference is seen in the offset in the
phase response.

The thing that remains and requires consideration is a direct feed-through term from input to output. This

proposed by Maciejowski (10 October 2000) which is based on a model in the standard form without the
feed-through coupling:

Algorithm 4.1 The sequence of actions in the MPC simulation
1: for k=0; k<Nsim; k++ do
2:
Obtain measurements from the plant, yk
3:
Estimate the ﬁltered current state vector,ˆxk|k and process noise, ˆ

Apply only the ﬁrst input, uk from the sequence to both the plant and the state estimator
7: end for
The author proposed a transformation helping to alleviate the problem that arises when dealing with
model in the form 4.3:
˜zk = zk −Duk
(4.7)
which leads to the standard form not depended any more on the current input.
Nevertheless the measurements from the plant need to be transformed to ˜yk in order to be consistent
with the transformed variable ˜zk, since any mathematical operation needs to be performed on operands of
the same type. That results in a complication since at the moment of measuring the output of a simulated
plant the current input is not yet known as it will be calculated once the current state and noise are estimated
based on the measurement yk. Hence it is not possible in this framework to calculate ˜yk.
Slightly different approach is proposed in the dissertation where instead of a Kalman ﬁlter in ﬁltering
mode, a stationary Kalman ﬁlter in prediction mode is utilised. Then it becomes possible to formulate
a problem in such a way to accommodate the feed-through term. Algorithm 4.2 lists the steps required

CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
and it can be noted that before measuring the output the current input is known because it is calculated in
the previous iteration of the algorithm. Graphically it is shown in the ﬂow chart 4.6.The Algorithm 4.2 is
executed over simulation horizon Nsim.

Optimal estimator
Kalman ﬁlter is an optimal estimator in a sense that it minimizes mean square error of the estimated
quantities under an assumption that all noises acting on a plant are Gaussian distributed and white. The
ﬁlter is recursive meaning that it incorporates measurements in each time step of its operation. It deduces
quantities of interest based on uncertain measurements.
As mentioned before the state estimator decided to be deployed here is in prediction mode. This is
so called predictive Kalman ﬁlter. It based on the model in the form 4.3. The equations describing it are
Jørgensen (2017):
ˆyk|k−1 = Cˆxk|k−1 + Duˆuk|k−1 + Dd ˆdk|k−1
(4.8)

Figure 4.6: A graphical visualization of the Algorithm 4.2
This above equation is for predicting output one step ahead based on the model matrices, previously predicted
states, the current input uk = ˆuk|k−1 and measured disturbance dk = ˆdk|k−1.
ek = yk −ˆyk|k−1
(4.9)
This so called innovation which is the difference between the measured output at current time k and predicted
output.
ˆxk+1|k = Aˆxk|k−1 + Buuk + Bddk + Kpek
(4.10)
This is the predicted state vector which is the output of the predictive Kalman ﬁlter. Kp is the Kalman gain
which pre-computed as it does not depend on measurement.
Summarizing, the ﬁlter makes output prediction and when the current measurement yk becomes available
it then makes the prediction of the states. When doing this it also utilises state estimates at time k made
based on the previous measurement at time k −1, ˆxk|k−1 and the present input uk.
Since the present input uk is not available yet, the obvious modiﬁcation is to store and utilize one step
delayed versions of the input and output of the model. The modiﬁcation to the equation is just to retreat time
indices. The ﬁnial equation set is presented:




ˆyk−1|k−2 = Cˆxk−1|k−2 + Duˆuk−1|k−2 + Dd ˆdk−1|k−2
ek−1 = yk−1 −ˆyk−1|k−2
ˆxk|k−1 = Aˆxk−1|k−2 + Buuk−1 + Bddk−1 + Kpek−1
(4.11)
As mentioned before the steady-state Kalman gain Kp for the stationary predictive Kalman ﬁlter can
be pre-computed outside of the main loop of the Algorithm 4.2 as follows. The gain is a function of error
covariance matrix of state estimate ˆxk given measurements up to and including {yt}t=k
t=1, Pk|k. Therefore
==PAGE:102==
80
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
calculating the gain can be done in two ways: either by iterating dynamic time-varying ﬁlter equations until
steady-state of error covariance matrix is reached or by using discrete algebraic Riccati equation (DARE).
The ﬁrst approach is to iterate the recursion for the predictive Kalman ﬁlter:
Pk+1|k = APk|k−1AT +GRwwGT −(APk|k−1CT +GRwv)(CPk|k−1CT +Rvv)−1(APk|k−1CT +GRwv)T
(4.12)
,where G = I matrix in dissertation is assumed to be identity matrix.
The above equation is derived from the recursions:
Re,k = CPk|k−1CT + Rvv
(4.13)
which is innovation covariance
Kp,k = (APk|k−1CT + GRwv)R−1
e,k
(4.14)
Pk+1|k = APk|k−1AT + GRwwGT −Kp,kRe,kKT
p,k
(4.15)
and assuming model in the form 4.3. Worth noting that in those equation the input matrix is not present.
The second approach stems from the ﬁrst one and observes that if the sequence of matrices {Pk|k−1}
converges for limk→∞to matrix P then the recursions can be written in form:
Re = CPCT + Rvv
(4.16)
Kp = (APCT + GRwv)R−1
e
(4.17)
P = APAT + GRwwGT −KpReKT
p
(4.18)
and
P = APAT + GRwwGT −(APCT + GRwv)(CPCT + Rvv)−1(ACT + GRwv)T
(4.19)
is called the discrete Riccati equation.
For the matrix to converge certain conditions must be satisﬁed depending on matrices Rwv and Rvv.
The most relevant case in the project is when Rwv = 0 and Rvv > 0. Then it follows that the sequence of
matrices {Pk|k−1} converges under the following sufﬁcient condition:
• pair (A, C) is detectable or all unobservable states are asymptotically stable
• pair

A, GR1/2
ww

is stabilizable or all uncontrollable states are asymptotically stable.
• P0|−1 ≥0
From the ﬁrst two it follows that the solution to Riccati equation is unique, positive semi-deﬁnite and
stabilizing - A −KpC is stable.

Figure 4.7: The bottom ﬁgure shows the measured disturbances acting on both the Kalman ﬁlter and the
plant. The middle ﬁgure shows the temperature setpoint and how the actual room temperature follows it.
The top one depicts the outputs of the plant - water ﬂow and room temperature.
In the top ﬁgure of 4.7 it is seen that the signals are strongly correlated and mirror each other. One of
the simpliﬁcation of the model becomes apparent, namely that the ﬂow rate can assume negative values.
That means the ﬂuid can move the opposite direction in the heating system. This is no possible in the real
system where the ﬂow is unidirectional so that the water ﬂows from the forward supply pipe and comes
back through the return pipe to the district heating utility. Moreover the ﬂow rate and the room temperature
should not mirror each other. A rather positive correlation should be observed so that when the ﬂow rate
increases the temperature in the room increases as well with the appropriate time delay of 10 samples. When
it decreases or goes to zero, the room temperature should start decreasing after the same delay. It can be
observed that the ﬂow rate can get negative in the model. That is because it was decided to use PID controller
as thermostat model as ﬁrst approximation to assure linearity. The hysteretic controller would make the
==PAGE:104==
82
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
model nonlinear entailing the need for formulation of nonlinear . One drawback of the solution is that the
negative ﬂow entails cooling effect in the model which does not exist in the real system. A negative ﬂow rate
cannot happen in the system because it would mean reversing the ﬂow of district heating. That simpliﬁcation
needs to be addressed in the future work.

2. PROBLEM FORMULATION
85
Regulator
The MPC is formulated as linear programming problem - objective function and constraints are a linear
function of decision variables:
min
˜Uk={
ˆ
Tset,k+j|k−1}N
j=0
N
X
j=1
ˆpT
k+j|k ˆEk+j|k−1( ˆmk+j|k−1)
s.t.
ˆxk+j+1|k−1 = Aˆxk+j|k−1 + Buˆuk+j|k−1 + Bd ˆdk+j|k−1
j = 0, 1, . . . , N −1
ˆzk+j|k−1 = Cˆxk+j|k−1 + Duˆuk+j|k−1 + Dd ˆdk+j|k−1
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
ˆmmin,k+j|k−1 ≤ˆmk+j|k−1 ≤ˆmmax,k+j|k−1
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
ˆymin,k+j|k−1 ≤ˆTi,k+j|k−1 ≤ˆymax,k+j|k−1
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
tl ≤ˆTsp,k+j|k−1 ≤tu
j = 0, 1, . . . , N
(4.34)
The solution to the problem is a sequence of temperature setpoints - {Tset}N
k=0 minimizing the cost
of the total predicted energy usage over the prediction horizon N. The objective function is multiplying
two factors. First one is a prediction of energy price k + j samples into the future, ˆpk+j|k−1, expressed in
[DKK/kWh]. The second one is the predicted energy usage made for the time k + j calculated based on
the sequence of past measurements up to and including time k −1, {yk}k−1
j=1. The optimization happens at
current time step k but it uses past measurements.
The minimized quantity is expressed as an estimate or a prediction because the optimization algorithm
calculates the control sequence ˜Uk = {Tset,k+j|k−1}N
j=0 at time k which minimizes the predicted quantity
and then only the ﬁrst control action is implemented on the plant. The problem speciﬁed 4.34 is repeated
every sample time.
The ﬁrst two equality constraints are modelling the dynamics of RFHS system controlled in closed loop
by the PID controller.
The next two consecutive inequality constrains are output constraints. The bounds can be speciﬁed
to be mmin = 0kg/sec and mmax = 0.01kg/sec for the entire prediction horizon or be some kind of a
proﬁle. The ﬁrst one will prevent the PID controller deﬁned in the framework of the model from sending a
negative control signal to the RFHS system. This is so called hard constraint which violation will result in
infeasibility and tried to be avoided at all cost. The upper bound value was determined looking at standard
ﬂow values in the type of system.
The constraint on room temperature can be elected to be ﬁxed ymin = 15◦C and ymax = 28◦C or
following a time-variable proﬁle. They have to be wider than the input constraints because of the unit gain in
steady state between the input and output of the model and a transient behaviour of the output happening
upon a change of the input.
The last constraint is a bound restriction on the control signal. In the project it is designed to be
tl = 19◦C and tu = 23◦C.
An important part of the regulator/optimizer is the objective function. Despite it being categorized as
part of the regulator and not a separate component it will be present as such next.
Objective function
This subsection presents how the objective function to be optimized in a sense of minimization, is
constructed. An economic MPC is related to operational costs which are to be minimized. In the case of
RFHS system which uses warm water from a district heating for the purpose of space heating, it is the heat
==PAGE:108==
86
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
energy which is being purchased. The price is expressed in [DKK/kWh] and the energy consumption for
the entire apartment is measured by the Kamstrup meter installed in the control cabinet of the apartment.
There is a need for estimating the energy consumption for a single room because as pointed out earlier
the thesis focuses on modelling and thermal control of only one room. It could be done using the meter’s
expression for calculating the energy usage.
According to the Kamstrup data sheet Kamstrup Multical 602 Data sheet:5810939 M1 GB 08.2016 (n.d.)
the energy is calculated by the standard prEN 1434-1:2014 using a formula:
Energy[J] = V [m3] · ∆Θ[K] · k[J/m3K],
(4.35)
where V is the supplied water volume, ∆Θ is the measured temperature difference and k = ρwcp is the
thermal coefﬁcient of water, ρw ≡1000kg/m3 is the density of water which depends on the temperature,
e.g. a 40◦C it is ρw = 999.2kg/m3 and cp = 4187J/kgK is the water speciﬁc heat.
The energy would ideally be computed as a function of model’s outputs. The prediction equation 4.33
relates the output with the optimized setpoints. Now how does the energy relate to the mass ﬂow rate of the
heat-conveying liquid being one of the model’s output?
To make the equation 4.35 a function of the mass ﬂow rate requires, in the ﬁrst step a replacement of the
water volume V with the volumetric ﬂow rate ˙V . Just that would yield a formula for the power. Conversion
to energy consumed during some period of time requires an additional multiplication by the sample time Ts,
with Ts being the said period. The idea is to calculate the energy consumption within Ts and multiply it by
the heat price for that period. Afterwards the total energy cost will be calculated by summing the constituent
costs over the predicton horizon N. The energy usage in single period is computed as:
Energy[J] = ˙V [m3/sec] · ∆Θ[◦C] · k[J/◦Cm3] · Ts[sec]
(4.36)
Next the volumetric water ﬂow in m3/sec needs to be expressed as a mass ﬂow rate in kg/sec.
After closing the loop with PID controller the input to RFHS model became an output of the controller
and made one of the outputs of the combined model. It has the units of kg/sec. The conversion from kg/sec
to m3/sec is:
V [m3] = m[kg]
ρw
=⇒
˙V [m3/sec] = ˙m[kg/sec]
ρw
(4.37)
Plugging that into equation 4.36 one has:
Energy[J] = ˙m[kg/sec]
ρw
· ∆Θ[◦C] · ρwcp[J/◦Cm3] · Ts[sec]
(4.38)
Knowing that 1J = 1Ws the energy expressed in units of kWh is:
Energy[kWh] =
1
3600 · 1000 ˙m[kg/sec] · ∆Θ[◦C] · cp[J/◦Cm3] · Ts[sec]
(4.39)
Currently in the apartment the forward and return water temperatures are not measured for each zone but
for the entire apartment. That implies making an assumption on the temperature difference in the pipes for a
room when the water ﬂow is non-zero. At the apartment level it is seen in the ﬁgure 3.7 that when the water
circulates the temperature difference is about 5◦C and therefore ∆Θ = 5◦C is assumed. The simpliﬁcation
is that the temperature drop across the piping system, ∆Θ is constant regardless of the thermodynamics of
the room. The ∆Θ would normally decay exponentially with the time because the ﬂoor layers would be
absorbing the energy form the circulating water. Without the temperature gradiant between the water and
ﬂoor layers no thermal energy exchange could happen. The system would reach a thermal equilibrium. The
formula 4.39 therefore can be written as:
Energy[kWh] = ˙m[kg/sec] · ϵ
(4.40)
,where ϵ =
1
3.6E + 06 · ∆Θ · ρwcp · Ts = const.
==PAGE:109==
2. PROBLEM FORMULATION
87
2.3

to 4.52.
The solver
The solver for the optimization problem 4.34 was chosen to be CPLEX by IBM, version 12.6.3.0. It has
an advantage of the speed for solving big problems compared to MATLAB’s counterparts. The formulation of
an optimization problem is slightly different than for MATLAB. For example linear programs are formulated
as inequality constraint problem with lower and upper bounds. Therefore any equality constraints must be
transformed adequately. This is easily done by setting equal lower and upper bounds. The intersection of
possible solutions will equate to the desired equality constraint.
By simulation the following issues in the implemented MPC algorithm have been discovered:
==PAGE:111==
2. PROBLEM FORMULATION
89
• As there is no cooling capacity in the real system the lower hard constraint on the mass ﬂow rate,
equal to zero has been speciﬁed in the MPC formulation. It has been decided so to avoid saturation
inside the model to yield a linear MPC. Nevertheless negative values can be observed in simulations.
• Any forces like solar radiation or ambient temperature variations can drive the controlled room
temperature out of the speciﬁed thermal comfort bounds. If it cannot be avoided by trying different
control actions this will render the underlying optimization problem infeasible and consequently lead
to a stall of MPC controller. Naturally those bounds need to be wide enough for that not to happen. A
thing which is not clear is that even by specifying comfort bounds which are assured not to be violated
the optimization problem still becomes infeasible unless the bounds are determined exceptionally
wide, e.g. 0◦C and 100◦C.
• Naturally any deviations from the forecasted disturbances compared to the actual disturbances can
cause the optimization to become infeasible by violating output constraints. An offset-free kalman
ﬁlter modiﬁcation can be introduced to the formulation which will take into account such events.
The above mentioned problems were addressed by introducing slack variables and deﬁning a soft constraint
problem. Unfortunately even though the constraints were relaxed it did not solved the issue. Possible culprit
of it could be poorly estimated model of the system with high uncertainties or a mistake in the deﬁnition of
constraints as diagnostic message from CPLEX suggests.
Soft constraint problem
As described already a possible solution to a problem of infeasibility of the optimization problem in the
face of tight output constraints could be deﬁning a soft constraint problem. The basic working principle of it
is that the problem is stipulated so that a penalty related to output constraint violation is brought into the
objective function. The optimizer will try to calculate such a sequence of optimized manipulated variables
so that the constraints are never violated but in case that it cannot be avoided it will be penalized in the
objective function according to user-speciﬁed weights discouraging such a situation.
Since the infeasibility seam to stem from the room temperature constraints since changing them has a
direct affect on the result of the simulation, it makes sense to redeﬁne them as soft constraints:
ˆymin,k+j|k−1 ≤ˆTi,k+j|k−1 ≤ˆymax,k+j|k−1
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
(4.54)
This equation is in fact two inequality constraints and will be dealt with in this way. Firstly an upper bound
slack variable is introduced su which cannot assume negative values and is added to the inequality constraint
whenever it is not true:



ˆTi,k+j|k−1 ≤ˆymax,k+j|k−1 + ˆsu,k+j|k−1
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
ˆsu,k+j|k−1 ≥0
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
(4.55)
Similarly for the lower bound constraints a lower bound slack variable is introduced sl:



ˆTi,k+j|k−1 ≥ˆymin,k+j|k−1 −ˆsl,k+j|k−1
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
ˆsl,k+j|k−1 ≥0
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
(4.56)
The equations 4.55 and 4.56 can be expressed respectively as:



Tk ≤Rmax,k + Su,k
Su,k ≥0
(4.57)

Input rate constraints
Input rate constraints could be also part of the formulation 4.52 but unfortunately were not implemented
in this project due to not satisfactory simulation results with a simplistically deﬁned MPC problem in the
ﬁrst place. Nonetheless the framework will be discussed here for future reference.
The idea is to allow the setpoints to make only discrete changes from the set {0, ±0.1◦C} within the
control horizon Nu. After that time they should stay unchanged for the rest of the prediction horizon N. The
constraints are deﬁned as follow



∆ˆTsp,k+j|k−1 ∈{0, ±0.1}
j = 0, 1, . . . , Nu −1
∆ˆTsp,k+j|k−1 = 0
j = Nu, . . . , N
(4.68)
The solution was inspired by the article Alberto Bemporad (1999) and the theory of mixed logical dynamical
(MLD) systems which are described by linear dynamic equations constraint with linear inequalities involving
both real and integer variables.
Firstly let us deﬁne the change ∆ˆTsp,k+j|k−1 in therms of the optimized variables ˆTsp,k+j|k−1. The
change is deﬁned as
∆ˆTsp,k+j|k−1 = ˆTsp,k+j|k−1 −ˆTsp,k+j−1|k−1
∀j = 0, 1, . . . , N
(4.69)

3. SIMULATION RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
93
3
Simulation results and discussion
Hard constraint problem
Figure 4.10 shows the hard constraint MPC simulation. With CPLEX software it lasted about 15 min
and due to output constraint violation only 13 days have been simulated. Conditions for the simulation were:
• ﬂow rate constraints: mmin = 0kg/sec, mmax = 1e12kg/sec
• room temperature constrains: ymin= 0◦C, ymax = 100◦C
• prediction horizon: N = 50
• simulation horizon: Nsim = 4032 which corresponds to 14 days but after 13 day the controller
stalled.
• initial room temperature: Ti,0 = 21◦C
• solver: CPLEX
• problem type: hard constraint
• starting point for Kalman ﬁlter: ˆx0|−1 = 0
The initial state vector x(k = 0) was calculated using the output constraint 4.80 and neglecting input and
measured disturbances in the following way:
z[k] = Czx[k] + Duu[k] + Ddd[k]
(4.80)
Since the model was estimated using black-box no physical meaning can be attached to the states. Knowing
the desired room temperature and having time-invariant model, hence ﬁxed Cz, the initial state is calculated
as:
x[k = 0] = Cz[2, :]−1z[k = 0]
(4.81)
,where k is the time instant. Only the second row of the matrix Cz is considered because the ﬁrst row of the
matrix or the ﬁrst value of the output z is the mass ﬂow rate. The second is the room temperature.
The ﬁgure 4.10 shows the hard constraint simulation. The ﬂow rate is restricted from below and yet
it can be seen it reaches negative values. The possible explanation is that the predicted output ﬂow rate is
restricted and any violation of that will cause infeasibility of the underlying mathematical problem. It must
be that those are not violated as the MPC controller does not stall when the measured ﬂow gets negative.
The predictions are made based on the perfect knowledge of disturbances prediction but the starting point,
the state vector ˆxk|k−1 is estimated using predictive Kalman ﬁlter as pointed out before. These predictions
have been proved to be quite accurate to within around 1◦C for the temperature and 0.5kg/sec for the ﬂow
rate. Therefore it is hard to justify that this discrepancy could cause the ﬂow rate to be overestimated of the
order of above 1kg/sec especially at the beginning and the end of the simulation where the ﬂow reaches a
value of −1.
Soft constraint problem
Below are the simulation results for the soft constraint problem where also the ﬂow rate constraints have
been relaxed as well 4.11. Conditions for the simulations were:
• ﬂow rate constraints: mmin = 0kg/sec, mmax = 1e12kg/sec

Figure 4.11: Soft constraint simulation.
the same constraints will be observed at its output in steady state excluding a transient behaviour. The ﬂow
rate reaches negative values sporadically and less frequently then in the case of hard constraints 4.10. This
happens whenever the PID controller counteracts disturbances driving the controlled variable away from
setpoint. Positive values of the ﬂow, on the other hand, are reaction of the controller to eliminate a positive
error on its input. The error is the difference between the setpoint and the measured and regulated signal
which in this case is the inside temperature. The disturbances acting on the model are the same as in the hard
constraint simulation 4.10 and yet the MPC controller reacts in much different way which is prominent in
the ﬂow rate log. It could be because the violation of ﬂow rate is happening and that is penalize heavily in
the objective function. Nevertheless any constraint violation in the hard constraint problem would stall the
MPC controller yet it did not happen. Therefore the predicted output did not violate the output constraints.
Both simulations should be the same until the stall of the MPC controller for the hard constraint simulation
occurred at the 14th day because no constraint violation happened. There is process and measurement noise
added but the seed of the noise is ﬁxed to 100. That way the simulations should be reproducible.
The MPC controller tends to favour mostly the lower bound of the temperature setpoint with some spikes
==PAGE:118==
96
CHAPTER 4. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
up to the upper bound which seam to be poorly correlated with dips in the heat price.
One issue with the simulation is that the control signal is going to the upper bound in a seemingly random
fashion, not correlated with the price signal. What is expected from these simulations is that control signal
will show correlation with the heat price. Whenever the price is relatively low the controller should take
action by requesting a rise in the room temperature to drive it to the upper limit so that in periods where the
energy price is high the controller does not have to request energy but rely on the accumulated energy in
the apartment’s thermal mass instead. The reason why the output constraints have to be wide and there is
no correlation between the price and the room temperature is an indicator of an issue which has not been
discovered in the time frame of the project and is to be addressed in future work.
==PAGE:119==
CHAPTER 5
Smart start
1
Introduction
The concept of smart start is based on an idea that a number of non-thermal household appliances can
offer delay ﬂexibility to the grid and achieve cost savings at the same time. Time-varying electricity prices
enable residents to save on the electricity bill as long as they schedule the energy consumption of some
devices to times when the electricity price is low. Since the price is determined by the supply and demand
principle those users can help to make the demand distribution closer to uniform by so called peak shaving
which is reducing the energy bought from the grid during peak demand hours. If smart start would be
implemented on a large scale and aggregated then this could reduce the grid congestion and DSO would not
need to increase the network capacity to meet the demand growth WP4.1 Identiﬁcation and characterization
of smart network services for urban network operation. (n.d.).
2
Smart start of appliances
The smart start interface in Frihavns Tårnet is programmed with ETS v5 software (available from knx.org
website). It expects to receive two signals from a cloud service. Those are:
• String with message to display in an operating panel mounted on the wall in each room
• Delay time for the appliance
It is possible to overwrite the smart start action by pressing a button on the control panel. If the panel
receives no signal within 30 min, it starts the appliance without any delay. It is some sort of watchdog system
in the panel which resets the counter upon received watchdog signal from the cloud service. It is envisioned
that the service should also provide the control action to thermostats from the MPC algorithm.
When user presses START (smart start) on a machine the energy meter detects current usage and closes
down a relay cutting off the power supply to the appliance. An interface controlling the relay expects to
receive the delay time otherwise it will open the relay starting up the appliance.
2.1
Physical set-up
An energy actuator - ABB type SE/3.16.1 is used to actuate three of the household appliances:
1. washing machine in the laundry room
2. tumble dryer in the laundry room, which is not included in ABB’s smart start
3. dish washer in the kitchen
97
==PAGE:120==
98
CHAPTER 5. SMART START
For all the machines one phase and a neutral wires are used to physically connect the module with those
machines. The communication with KNX system is enabled. The module can switch independently at most
3 electrical loads and all of them can be controlled via the KNX separately. The actuator calculates the total
energy consumption of the devices it controls and measures:
1. active power
2. current AC
3. voltage AC
4. frequency
3
Problem formulation
The smart start problem is formulated in a similar way as in C. Chen (2013). Two cases are deﬁned for
operation of a machine:
• one task operation - the operation of a machine is treated as a continuous in time, single task
• multi-task operation - the operation of a machine is subdivided into tasks and dependence on other
machines is present referred to later as inter- and intra-appliance dependencies
The intra-appliance dependency has been deﬁned in the article on example of a washing machine. The
operation of a washing machine is constituted of several sequential sub-tasks like washing, rinsing, spinning.
Some delay between those are allowed.
The inter-appliance dependency is a reliance of a machine on other appliances. An example could be
tumble drying clothes after washing them. The tumble dryer is not considered in smart start in the project.
Firstly a situation with one task is studied. That means the operation of an appliance is regarded as one
uninterrupted block and is not subdivided into tasks. Afterwards it is extended to multi-task operation as
suggested in the article.
The optimization should be called only when user requests it. Otherwise the appliance should power on
immediately after push of a button. It is up to the user to select the delay ﬂexibility to take advantage of the
low prices periods. It is understood as the time delay between the user’s request and the start of a device as
well as the time in between the tasks.
4
One task operation
In this section a situation is presented where n appliances request start at the same time. It could be for
example a dishwasher, a washing machine and a tumble dryer.
==PAGE:121==
4. ONE TASK OPERATION
99
4.1
Cost minimization
The appliance scheduling is a ﬁxed-horizon optimization problem formulated as cost minimization. It is
solved for each smart start request event of an appliance individually.

the right hand side of this constraint is Di, which is a vector of delay ﬂexibilities for each of the
appliances.
The important notice is that the scheduling has to happen within the prediction horizon N. That means
the start-up and shut-down actions for each appliance have to happen within that time. The equations do not
allow the situation where there is a receding horizon MPC strategy applied and a device is started in one
time instance and continue to stay ON at the next one when the new optimization starts. That functionality is
to be extended in the future work by possible implementing a memory element keeping track of the tasks
started and ﬁnished. Moreover, if several events happen at the same time, they are allowed to be executed at
any moment independently of each other, possibly overlapping.
Simulation results
Now the algorithm is tested in simulation using MATLAB solver for solving mixed integer linear
programming - INTLINPROG. The problem does contain only binary variables and hence is a binary linear
program. The conditions for the simulation were:
• 3 appliances requested smart start scheduling at time 0.
==PAGE:125==
4. ONE TASK OPERATION
103
• Simulation horizon N = 7200 min.
• Simulation step Delta = 10 min.
• Overall delay ﬂexibility D = N min for all devices. It means that the execution can be delayed until
the end of the simulation horizon if ﬁnancially beneﬁcial.
• Minimum operation time T ONmin = [500; 300; 100] min
• Maximum operation time T ONmax = [540; 340; 140] min
Figure 5.1 shows how the operation of two of the appliances was scheduled to the second dip in the
electricity price, despite the fact that the ﬁrst period contained negative prices and could provide cost beneﬁts
for the user. It is because that period lasted quite short and the operation time for those devices was longer.
It turned out that more ﬁnancial beneﬁts were achieved by delaying the energy consumption. On the other
hand the 3rd device required minimum operating time 100 min, exactly as long as the negative price dip
period. Therefore the operation of that device was scheduled at that time. The operating time of the other
two devices was equal to their minimum time required. As noted earlier if the negative price period would
last longer the device would be scheduled to make full use of it by operating up to the maximum operating
time.

5
Multi-task operation
5.1
Cost minimization
CPLEX 12.6.3.0 optimization solver is used to solve the Mixed Binary Linear Programming optimization
problem. CPLEX Connector for MATLAB is used as an interface to the solver from MATLAB R2015b.
For the purpose of formulating the problem, an event is deﬁned as user’s power consumption request
C. Chen (2013). It can encompass a few machines related to each other in some sense. For example washing
machine and a dryer have a link with one another because if those devices are present in an apartment then
after washing, the clothes need to get dried. The optimization problem is formulated for only one such event.
Nevertheless an event can include more then one appliance as stated above. Internally it will be represented
as a sequence of tasks.
The problem for multi-task case is formulated in a similar manner as for single-task case with the
difference that instead of simulating many independent appliances each operating in a single block for which
start is triggered at the same time, now the simulation will be for only one event with independent tasks
running in sequence. The following equations state the binary linear programming problem:

objective function the energy cost should be minimized. In fact as it is shown 5.16 in the objective function
the price is in [DKK/MWh] multiplied by the power in [W] which is incorrect. Formally the objective
function should look like:

The formula can be implemented in MATLAB and represented in a matrix form by using permutation

Π : {1, . . . , (STi · N)} →{0 · STi + 1, STi + 1, 2 · STi + 1, . . . , (N −1) · STi + 1,

{π(1), . . . , π(STi · N)}

4:

5:
else

7:
end if
8:
end for
9: end for

==PAGE:135==
CHAPTER 6
Conclusions and Discussion
The chapter includes the conclusions and discussion on the three areas of focus.
1
Model of thermodynamics
The study explored different modelling possibilities given the two distinguishably different data sets
made available for the use at two points in time. The experimental data was collected by conducting three
experiments on 13th, 15th and 17th of March 2017. The ﬁrst one was a test of the communication system,
logging possibilities and measurement data points available. The second was examining the step response
and the third one was a PRBS with the guess of of time constants of the apartment. Afterwards scripts for
extracting the useful information and presenting it were developed in R language. The operational data from
January 2017 was available on the 15th of June 2017 and processed using MATLAB scripts also developed
in the thesis. This project aimed at achieving a thermo-dynamical model of an apartment for the use in
EMPC.
1.1
One-zone model of an apartment
Interpreting the collected data was not immediately possible after recording them because they required
some processing in order to retrieve meaningful information. Therefore a chance was missed to interpret the
data and draw conclusions about the thermal behaviour of the apartment before re-designing the identiﬁcation
experiment to excite the dominating dynamics.
Using the data collected from the third experiment, designed to excite every zone with the same excitation
signal, to estimate one-zone model of the apartment, proved to be not scientiﬁcally reasonable. The reason
is that the practical limitations for conducting the experiments required controlling of the electro-valves
through the non-linear hysteretic thermostats by manipulating the temperature set-points. Once the indoor
temperature in a zone exceeded the upper limit of the set-point it was no longer feasible to control the
corresponding radiator electro-valve of that zone. This resulted in non-consistent excitation signals for the
valves. It is not reasonable to aim at one-zone model under such conditions. This is conﬁrmed by the results
presented.
Two kinds of models were estimated: two non-linear and one linear. The validation results showed that
the linear model sufﬁced to capture the dynamics of the RFHS according to residual analysis test but the
same was not conﬁrmed through the simulation of the output of the model in MATLAB&SIMULINK. It
was found that the model is uncontrollable from the heat control value input. The choice of the input to the
model was a reason why the such a model could not be suitable for MPC. Ideally a mass ﬂow rate in units of
113
==PAGE:136==
114
CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSIONS AND DISCUSSION
[kg/sec] should be used instead of the heat control value in [%] but the data point was not available during
the experiment due to Kamstrup energy meter failure.
1.2
Model of one room
Once the operational data was ready for processing, it was decided to develop a single zone model of
the selected apartment due to natural mismatch in excitation signals to the control valves stemming from a
usual use of the apartment. The data from January to May 2017 was analysed to ﬁnd an apartment or single
room without the missing measurements, with the RFHS activated and containing the required signals for
identiﬁcation including:
• inside temperature and their set-points
• water ﬂow rate
• valve’s activation status (equivalent to the heating control value within the time of 3 min due to
electro-valve’s actuation time)
Finally a room in the apartment 35 on the 11th ﬂoor was found to meet all the criteria. The data is from
January 2017. The analysis of the data from 6 apartments starting from February on, conﬁrmed that the
RFHS was deactivated. A probable cause, just like in other Nordhavn building, is the centrally-controlled
ventilation system whose set-point is set to 21◦C and concises with the thermal comfort of inhabitants.
Many of them complained about not being able to lower the temperature which was higher than 21◦C.
The building thermal capacity is very high and it looses temperature very slowly. One of the previously
mentioned experiment conﬁrmed that 6 hours are needed to lower the temperature by 0.5◦C to 24◦C with
all the windows closed.
A black-box approach was taken because the linear grey-box model did not pass the validation test
where simulated output from the model was compared with the measured output. A few black-box models,
of different structure, were tested and the selected one is in a state space form. It is a second-order linear
model. It was estimated using PEM but the estimation process stopped preliminary because it reached the
maximum iteration number. This is attributed to the replacement weather data measured by DTU Byg’s
weather station in Lyngby rather than directly at the Frihavns Tårnet. The solar radiation has big inﬂuence
on the indoor climate and is very dependent on the cloudy conditions of a place. Nevertheless the validation
stage conﬁrmed that the model captures the general trend in the data and is controllable from the volumetric
ﬂow rate input and hence was chosen for the EMPC.
1.3
Thermostat model
Having estimated the RFHS model, it needed to be integrated with the model of local controller in the
closed-loop. The accurate modelling of hysteresis effect proved to be challenging. Another reason for
opting out of it is that the hysteresis function is non-linear. Taking an inspiration from the literature about
similar systems as the one dealt with in the project, lead to the decision to use a linear PID controller in
the simulations instead. Another motivation is that such a control scheme can be selected in the thermostat
module of ABB as well.
One of the issue which made itself apparent was the fact that the PID controller allows for negative
control action, being the volumetric ﬂow rate, to the model of RFHS. That is not acceptable in the real system.
In EMPC simulation, an attempt was made to prevent it, by setting hard lower bound constraint on the
combined model’s output which is the mass ﬂow rate (scaled volumetric ﬂow rate). Nevertheless the actual
ﬂow rate was getting negative. It should be noted that the hard constraints only apply to the predicted output
==PAGE:137==
2. ECONOMIC MPC
115
which is not necessarily the same as the actual output. In the simulation it was assumed that the EMPC has
the perfect knowledge of the system excluding the noise disturbances on the process and measurements. The
noise could be the reason for discrepancy between the predicted and actual outputs. Lastly, it should be
pointed out that if the EMPC would be implemented in the real system or tested in the Hardware-In-the-Loop
set-up, the PID’s range would be limited and adjusted to the actuator range and would not allow for negative
control actions. Therefore it is believed that better simulation results could be achieved by implementing
the model in SIMULINK and enabling saturation in the PID controller block. The SIMULINK ﬁle should
then be invoked from MATLAB script with EMPC algorithm. Naturally integral windup is likely to occur
because of the actuator limitations and anti-windup techniques should be considered. The linear MPC can
only be used in the linear range of PID operation. In saturation, the control action of PID will be ﬁxed to
"fully open" or "fully closed" and the system will be uncontrollable in these states but stable, regarding this
particular system.
2
Economic MPC
A linear EMPC algorithm was designed using the estimated linear model with direct feed-through from
input to output. This term excluded the use of the standard formulation of MPC. Instead, a part of it, the
estimator, had to be formulated as predictive KF. The algorithm used to calculate the optimal temperature
set-points was presented in details in the project.
The simulation results showed that due to some issue the output constraints cannot be set very tight,
otherwise the simulation resulted in violating some constraints. The EMPC was extended by adding slack
variables forming a so called soft constraint problem in an attempt to resolve the issue of stalling controller.
Such a situation and solution to it was also presented in Daniel Esteban Morales Bondy (2012). Any output
constraint violation would not result in principle in the stall of the controller, having the soft constraints
deﬁned, but instead the control actions leading to such violations would be penalized in the objective function,
giving an incentive to the optimizer, calculating the control actions, not to let it happen.
The simulation over a two weeks period showed that the heat price signal was not correlated with the
control action - set-point changes. In principle, at low price periods the EMPC should command the local
controllers a higher temperature set-points to accumulate the energy in the building mass unless it would lead
to thermal comfort limits violation. Conversely when the heat price is high the controller should command
the control valves to shunt down to save energy by making use of the accumulated energy.
The simulation results should be interpreted carefully. The estimated model has a unit gain in steady
state and incorporates a PID controller allowing for negative ﬂow rates. The ﬂow rate determines the cost of
the total energy usage. Any change of temperature set-point will affect the ﬂow rate. An action of lowering
the set-point will cause the ﬂow rate to assume negative values to bring the temperature down fast, which
will lead to an instantaneous objective function value being negative which means making proﬁt through that
action. Negative ﬂow rates are not feasible in the system and therefore a hard constraint was put on the it in
the EMPC. Nevertheless the actual mass ﬂow rate was getting negative.
3
Smart start
In the thesis an algorithm for smart start of household appliances was presented for two cases: single-task
and multi-task operation. The former can be perceived as a simple delayed start of an appliance at the
moment in time coinciding with the lowest electricity prices. The latter is a more sophisticated variation
of the ﬁrst. It makes use of the fact that an operation of a device can be subdivided into several tasks. An
example is the washing machine whose operation consists of rinsing, dispensing of detergent, washing and
==PAGE:138==
116
CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSIONS AND DISCUSSION
spinning cycles in general. Assuming the device can be directly controlled and allowing for some time
ﬂexibility between the cycles, cost savings or providing services to the electricity grid are possible.
==PAGE:139==
CHAPTER 7
Future work
1
Modelling
In the future work it is recommended to improve the linear model of the room, using both grey- and
black-box approaches. The grey-box approach is favoured due to physical interpret-ability of its states. Also
one-zone model should be developed. For that purpose a few things should be considered:
1. A weather station with solar irradiance sensor should be installed directly on the building or in its
close proximity.
2. Temperature sensors for each of the control valves should be placed in the outlet manifold if multiple-
zone model of an apartment or a model of a single zone is decided. That will eliminate a need to
estimate the return water temperature of each zones using a non-linear function.
3. An identiﬁcation experiment should be allowed to be conducted where the electro-valves can be
directly controlled (for example in the way described in the thesis 1.4).
4. The ventilated air to the apartment needs to be considered as a disturbance in the thermodynamic
model. It has a big inﬂuence on the system. One needs to know the exact air ﬂow rate and its
temperature.
5. Thermal dependencies between the zones.
The focus should be on the dynamics of the ﬂoor as is pointed out in the studied literature and the room’s
dynamics could be either static or a ﬁrst-order dynamic ODE.
Moreover a non-linear model of thermostat should be explored assuring that the mass ﬂow rate never
gets negative. In the project the buﬁrcation model of hysteresis was presented. It requires additional focus
before making use of it. An alternative is a saturation of PID controller used in the thesis.
2
Economic MPC
A non-linear MPC will be required if a saturation of the mass ﬂow rate in the model is to be considered.
Alternatively the model can be linearized in few operating points and the resulting models can be switched
between depending on the current operating point, in the MPC algorithm.
The control strategy presented in Honglian Thybo and Thybo (2007) could be very interesting for the
apartment’s user from their thermal comfort point of view and should be considered in the future work.
Apart from that the merge of the EMPC with the smart start algorithm should be aimed for. The idea
is that one predictive controller decides on the temperatures set-points of thermostats and the delay of
117
==PAGE:140==
118
CHAPTER 7. FUTURE WORK
non-thermal appliances. The smart start should be adopted to the receding horizon strategy where the START
and STOP actions are not restricted to occur in the same prediction horizon.
The wind balancing should be taken into account as the smart start application. The concept would be
effective if a great number of apartments is grouped in an aggragator. The appliances should be directly
optimally dispatched and not indirectly dispatched via the dynamic energy price signals.

Closing the loop through a PID controller
A PID controller can be converted to state-space form:



ηk + 1 = Acηk + Bcek
mk = Ccηk + Dcek,
(6)
where mk is the control signal being an output of the controller and an input to RFHS model, direct feed-
through from input to output Dc ̸= 0, an error ek = yref,k −yk is the difference between a reference and an
measured output which in this instance is the output from RFHS.
Having the model of RFHS in the form 5 and the controller model feedback loop is closed from the
output of RFHS model, yk to the input to the PID controller. The controller is connected with the RFHS
through mk signal which in the dissertation stands for the ﬂow rate of circulating water in the RFHS.

Fourth Appendix
Sorting ﬁles
The following MATLAB source code was used to sort ﬁles containing one-month data from all apartments,
into ﬁles with measurements of a particular KNX data point and IP gateway address (apartment).
1
%% Author: Jakub Krzysztof Rodek (s151836)
2
% Date: 28-05-2017
3
%https://se.mathworks.com/help/matlab/import_export/import-large-text-files.html
4
%%
5
file = 'april.csv';
6
ds = datastore(file,'TreatAsMissing','NA',...
7
'Delimiter',',','NumHeaderLines',0 );
8
%%
9
ds.VariableNames{1} = 'Date_Time';
10
ds.VariableNames{2} = 'Measurement';
11
ds.VariableNames{3} = 'Gateway_IP';
12
ds.VariableNames{4} = 'KNX_group_addr';
13
14
%%
15
ds.SelectedVariableNames = {'Gateway_IP'};
16
preview(ds)
17
18
%% to allow parallel pool to start on the local machine if Mpiexec
19
% misconfigured
20
distcomp.feature( 'LocalUseMpiexec', true )
21
22
%% all available gateway IPs
23
outds = mapreduce(ds, @IPMapFun, @IPReduceFun);
24
%%
25
readall(outds);
26
%%
27
A
= read(outds)
28
%apartments = unique(Value{1});
29
char(A.Value{1}) %all Gateway IPs
30
31
%%
32
reset(ds);
33
ds.SelectedVariableNames = ds.VariableNames;
34
preview(ds)
35
%% close all open files
181
==PAGE:204==
182
FOURTH APPENDIX
36
fclose('all');
37
%% write data to txt files w.r.t. gateway IPs
38
fol_name = 'april';
39
40
for i = 1:length(A.Value{1}) % i is the index to the current gateway IP
41
mkdir(['./' fol_name '/'],['gateway_' num2str(i)]); %make a separate
42
%
directory for each apartment data
43
reset(ds); %reset to the beginning chunk of the data because
44
% it scans the same fiel for different IP addresses
45
while hasdata(ds)
46
[T, info] = read(ds); % read the next chunk
47
T = T(strcmp((A.Value{1}(i)),(T.Gateway_IP)),: );
48
filename = [fol_name '/gateway_' num2str(i) '/newfile_',...
49
num2str(info.Offset), '.txt'];
50
writetable(T, filename,'WriteVariableNames', false); % write to a file
51
end
52
end
53
54
%% look for unique KNX Group address in gateway_1
55
% base_dir = 'D:/Christian Brandt task/tables/gateway_1/';
56
57
base_dir = '\\tsclient/D/Christian Brandt task/april/gateway_11/';
58
% base_dir = 'D:/Christian Brandt task/february/gateway_5/';

62
newds.VariableNames{2} = 'Measurement';
63
newds.VariableNames{3} = 'Gateway_IP';
64
newds.VariableNames{4} = 'KNX_group_addr';
65
66
preview(newds)
67
%% look for unique KNX group addrs in data from a given apartment
68
newds.SelectedVariableNames = 'KNX_group_addr';
69
outds2 = mapreduce(newds, @GroupAddrMapFun, @GroupAddrReduceFun);
70
71
%% View the unique KNX group addrs

readall(outds2)

D = read(outds2)

D.Value{1}

length(D.Value{1})
